FOR OFFICIAL USE ONLY
DEFINITIONS
Crude Birth Rate:   Number of live births per year per 1,000 of
population.
Crude Death Rate:   Number of deaths per year per 1,000 of population.
Rate of Natural
Increase:          Difference between crude birth and crude death rate;
usually expressed as a percentage.
..
Rate of Population
Growth:            Rate of natural increase adjusted for (net) migration,
and expressed as a percentage of the total population
of a given year.
General Fertility
Rate:              Number of live births per year per 1,000 women, aged
15-49 years.
Age-Specific
Fertility Rates:  Number of live births to women in a given age group
per 1,000 women in the same age group, iQ a given
year. It is usually calculated for 5-year age
groups.
Tctal Fertility
Rate:              The average number of children that would be born
per woman if she were to live to the end of her
childbearing years, and bear children according
to a given set of age-specific fertility rates.
The Total Fertility Rate often serves as an est4-
mata of the average number of children per family.
Gross Reproduction
Rate:              This is the same as the Total Fertility Rate, but
refers to the number of daughters a woman would
have under prevailing fertility patterns.
Net Reproduction
Rate:              The aumber of daughters a woman would have under
prevailing fertility and mortality patterns, who
would survive to the mean age of childbearing.
Infant Mortality
Rate:              Annual number of deaths of infants under 1 year
per 1,000 live births during the same year.
This document has a restricted distribution and may be used by recipients only in the performance
of their official duties. Its contents may not otherise be disclosed without World Bank authonzation.



DEFINITIONS (Cont'd)
Maternal Mortality
Rate:              Number of maternal deaths per 1,000 births attri-
butable to pregnancy, childbirth, or puerperal
complications (i.e., within six weeks following
childbirth).
Life Expectancy:    Average number of years expected to be lived by
children born in the same year if mortality rates
for each age/sex group remain the same in the
future.
Dependency Ratio:   Number of people of 14 years or under, plus people
65 years or over, divided by the population aged
15 to 64 years.

I. POPULATION GROWTH, FAMILY HEALTH AND SOCIO-ECONOMIC DEVELOPMENT
A. Introduction
1.01      In January 1978, the estimated population of Bangladesh was about
84.8 million. According to Government sources, the natural rate of growth
of population has declined from 2.8% in 1975 1/ to about 2.5% in 1978, owing
to a fall in the birth rate from 47 to an estimated level of 44 during this
period. 2/ This decline, if confirmed, is significant but not sufficiently
substantial to make an impact on socio-economic conditions.
1.02      A child born in Bangladesh today faces prospects of a grim life.
Life expectancy at birth is about 42 years; one-fifth of children die before
the age of five. Tetanus, diptheria and diarrhea are major causes of infant
and child mortality; scabies, respiratory infections and worm infestations,
are prevalent. About 16% of the children from 0-5 years are wasted and
stunted because of acute and chronic malnutrition and the growth of another
58% is stunted by chronic undernutrition. 3/ Because one-third of pregnant
and lactating mothers suffer from calorie deficiency, their children are weak
and vulnerable. Communicable diseases such as malaria, tuberculosis, leprosy,
and diarrhea account for a large proportion of morbidity and mortality. About
90% of the people live in the rural areas where, although agriculture is the
main occupation, about 45% of families are landless. No less than one-third
of the labor force is either unemployed or underemployed. Per capita income
of about US$90 (in 1977) is one of the lowest in the world, while the depen-
dency ratio of about 95 is one of the highest in the world. Therefore, an
average family of six members must survive on about US$45 per month; for the
many families whose incomes are below the average, one full meal a day is a
luxury. Only about 23% of the population is literate; the illiterates are
generally poorer and have larger families. 4/
1/   The population program, disrupted after independence, was re-established
in 1975, which can be considered as the base year for measuring program
performance. The first population project, supported by IDA, Australia,
Canada, the Federal Republic of Germany, Norway, Sweden and the United
Kingdom, also became effective in September 1975.
2/   Subject to confirmation by the Census of 1981 and a prevalence
survey proposed for late 1979.
3/   Source:  Nutrition Survey of Rural Bangladesh conducted by the Institute
of Nutrition and Food Sciences, Dacca University, December 1977, p. 198.
4/   The Bangladesh Fertility Survey (BFS), conducted as part of the World
Fertility Survey in 1975-76, showed a clear inverse relationship between
education and fertility.



-2-
1.03      A continuing high birth rate will exacerbate these desperate
conditions, and the prospects for better health and education and of conse-
quent improvement in welfare will become even more uncertain. Realizing the
gravity of the threat, the Government of Bangladesh (GOB) has accorded popula-
tion control the highest priority in its development program and proposes to
attack the problem by means of more concerted efforts to: (a) improve health
and education; (b) reduce infant, child and maternal mortality; (c) raise
the socio-economic status of women through vocational training programs;
(d) change the outlook of the rural population by the use of mass media;
(f) deliver contraceptive services to people at home; and (g) enlist community
support for population planning.
B. Demographic Trends
1.04      Bangladesh's population has doubled since 1951.  The rate of growth,
which is close to the natural rate since migration is negligible, rose from
1% in the early fifties to about 2.8% in the mid-seventies, mainly because
of a rapid decline in the death rate. Data are summarized in the following
table and details are given in Annex 7, T-1 and T-2.
Population       Natural
Size (in        Rate of                                Total
Year               millions)    Population        Birth       Death      Fertility
Growth        Rate        Rate         Rate
1951 Census          42.6             1.0         47.8        38.2          -
1961 Census          53.4             2.0         50.2        28.4         7.3
1974 Census          76.4             2.8         47.4        19.4         7.0
1975 BFS             78.5             2.8         47.0        19.0         6.3
1978 est.            84.8             2.5         44.0        19.0          -
1.05      The Bangladesh Fertility Survey (BFS) estimated that in 1975 the
birth rate was 47 and the death rate 19. These figures are close to the
census estimates for 1974. Although no reliable data for 1978 are available,
the GOB estimates that the birth rate may have fallen to 44 1/ and that the
death rate may have remained constant at 19.
1.06      Due to constant high fertility and mortality in the past, the pro-
portion of population below 15 years has remained almost constant at over
45% of the population during 1961 and 1974 (Annex 7, T-3). The average age
of marriage in 1974 was 15.9 years for females and 24.0 years for males.
1/ The basis of this estimate is an increase in the contraceptive prevalence
rate as revealed by surveys conducted during 1978.



C. Demographic Prospects and Implications
1.07      The implications of population growth can be analyzed by considering
the consequences of two assumptions about the decline of fertility over the
next seventy years. The first, and optimistic, assumption is that the two-
child family will become the norm by the turn of the century or, in technical
terms, that the net reproduction rate (NRR) will fall to one by 2000-2005.
The second, and perhaps more realistic, assumption is that the two-child
family norm (i.e., a NRR of one) will not be reached until 2030-35. To assess
the implications of different rates of decline in fertility, the same decline
of mortality is assumed in both cases, so that life expectancy would increase
in both cases from about 48 years at present to 54 years by the end of the
century, and reach the level of developed countries (70 years) in 2085.
1.08      On the first assumption, the natural rate of growth of population
would decline to below 1.5% by 2000; the population would be more than twice
the present size by 2030, and would become stationary at about 212 million by
2165. On the second assumption, the natural rate of growth would remain above
2% until the end of the century, and the population would be nearly three
times its present size in 2030, becoming stationary at more than 330 million--
four times its present size--by 2165. Thus a delay of 30 years in reducing
fertility to the replacement level would add more than 100 million to
Bangladesh's population (more than its present size) before it became
stationary. Data are given in Annex 7, T-4.
1.09      Although the implications of population growth for Bangladesh's
development prospects under the optimistic first assumption are serious
enough, those under the second are staggering. It is evident that Bangladesh's
chances of meeting even the basic needs of its population will dwindle with
every delay in reducing the rate of growth of population. For example, even
on the first assumption, arable land per person will decline from the present
meager 0.3 acre to 0.2 acre by 2000, and will fall as low as 0.18 acres under
the second assumption. At the same time, annual food grain requirements, even
at the current low level (16 ozs per day), will rise from 14 million tons in
1975 to 27 million tons in 2000, compared with less than 24 million tons under
the first assumption. An unprecedented increase in yield per acre would there-
fore be necessary to maintain consumption at a subsistence level (Annex 7, T-5).
Under the first assumption, an investment of about US$14 billion would be
required by the turn of the century to provide schools for every child in the
5-14 age group. The required investment would be at least one billion higher
under the second assumption. The labor force would increase from about 26
million in 1973 to about 89 million in 2050 under the first assumption, and
to 113 million under the second assumption. Therefore, if a NRR of one were
attained by 2000-2005, 63 million more jobs would be needed by 2050; if, on
the other hand, the NRR did not fall to one until 2030-2035, over 87 million
more jobs would be needed. Given the low rate of economic growth in the past
and the extent of current unemployment and underemployment, it would clearly
be impossible to provide employment on the scale called for by either assump-
tion (Annex 7, T-6).



- 4 -
II.   NATIONAL POPULATION AND HEALTH POLICIES, STRATEGIES AND PROGRAMS
A. Policies
Population
2.01      Voluntary efforts to promote family planning during the 1950s
led to the adoption of a national program in the early 1960s.  In 1965, the
Government set itself the task of reducing the rate of growth of population
from 3% in 1965 to 2.5% in 1970 by reducing the birth rate from 50 to 40 and
the death rate from 20 to 15. At the same time, it established a network of
family planning clinics administered by an autonomous Family Planning Board,
independent of the health service. The program suffered from weaknesses of
policy and administration and a lack of full-time field workers, and conse-
quently had little effect on fertility. The BFS of 1975-76 showed no sig-
nificant change in fertility since 1966-68. The total fertility rate was
estimated at about 6.3 by BFS and 6.0 by the National Impact Survey (NIS)
conducted in 1966-68. However, the program sharpened public awareness of
population issues and modern contraceptive methods, and thus created a latent
demand for family planning. According to BFS, about 82% of the respondents
knew about family planning, compared with the NIS figure of 62%.
2.02      In 1972-73, immediately after Bangladesh became independent, the
family planning activities came to a virtual halt. Program performance fell
to an all-time low and its methods and organization were found unsuitable.
One of the main issues at this time was whether the vertical family planning
program should be continued, or whether health and family planning services
should be integrated. It was against this background that the GOB prepared
its First Five-Year Plan (1973-78), which called for a decline in fertility
to replacement level by the end of the century. A broad-based multi-sectoral
population program described in para 2.06 was thus formulated. The target was
drastically revised in 1976 with a view to attaining a NRR of one as early as
1985 and thus containing population size to about 120 million by the end of
the century. Subsequently, the GOB realized that this revised target was
too ambitious and set 1990 as the date for reaching a NRR of one. Even this
target is too ambitious in relation to program capacity and socio-economic
conditions, as shown in paras. 2.39-2.43.
2.03      These ambitious targets reflect GOB's realization of the need for
drastic action. Based on the progress of the program to date and the experi-
ence of other countries, it would appear unlikely that these goals can be
reached. It should be recognized that unrealizable targets are not only
frustrating to program staff but, more seriously, jeopardize development
itself if economic planning is based on them. The GOB is, however, consider-
ing various unconventional measures during the Second Five-Year Plan period
(1980-85) to achieve an unprecedented decline in fertility. These measures
include a four-year birth moratorium to be implemented through community
participation, giving awards to successful villages, providing special



- 5--
health care to these villages, raising the legal age at marriage, devising new
ways of extending family planning services, expanding the participation of the
private sector in the family planning program and sanctioning flexible finan-
cial and administrative procedures for PCFPD.
2.04      To implement the current policy effectively, the GOB proposes to
prepare a plan of action geared to achieving the demographic targets to
be measured in terms of contraceptive prevalence rates during the next five
years.  This plan of action will be used as a management tool to review and
monitor program progress.
Health
2.05      As a result of the control of epidemics alone, the death rate
declined drastically from about 38 in 1951 to 19 in 1974. The first Five-
Year Plan, pending the census results, assumed a death rate of 17 in 1973 and
envisaged a decline to 15 in 1978. This goal has now been postponed until
1985 on the basis of an analysis made by the World Health Organization (WHO)
in its country health programming exercise. 1/ (The reduction in deaths
from various causes necessary to achieve this decline are given in Annex 7,
T-7.) The Ministry of Health and Population Control (MOHPC) is now planning
to set up a data collection system and to lay down targets for reductions in
mortality and morbidity. A health policy statement, currently under prepa-
ration, in connection with the Second Five-Year Plan is expected to be final-
ized by July 1979. The objectives of this health policy are: (a) provision
of primary health care to meet priority health needs; (b) development of
health manpower to render primary health care; (c) integration of the program
for communicable disease control with general health services; (d) provision
of specialized services in health care institutions; (e) reorientation of
drug production and procurement--linking availability with health priorities;
(f) promoting industrial and school health; (g) provision of safe drinking
water and waste disposal facilities; and (h) development of a health informa-
tion system.
B. Strategies
Population
2.06      The current strategies were adopted during the first Five-Year
Plan. The Plan envisaged that by 1978:
(a)  the family planning program would be integrated with the
health service;
(b)  health and family planning services would be delivered in rural
areas through home visits by 10,000-11,000 teams of field workers,
with one team (one male and one female) for every 8,000 people, and
one rural health center and about three subcenters would be estab-
lished in each of the 356 rural thanas (about 160 rural health
centers and 180 subcenters existed in the country at that time);
1/   WHO--Country Health Programming in Bangladesh--Programme Proposal,
August 1977, Dadca.



- 6 -
(c)  educational and motivational efforts would be made by all
ministries that maintain wide public contact, e.g., the Minis-
tries of Agriculture; Education; Labor and Social Welfare; Local
Government, Rural Development and Cooperatives; and Information
and Broadcasting;
(d)  voluntary family planning organizations would be given
encouragement and support;
(e)  the GOB would consider applying strong incentives after the
second child, legalizing abortion and introducing measures for
the emancipation of women; and
(f)  a central Population Planning Division would be established to
coordinate the multi-sectoral population program and population
policy would be formulated by a National Population Council.
It is to the GOB's credit that it has acted on all the above strategies,
naturally with differing degrees of completeness and success as described
below.
2.07      In 1973, the family planning program was integrated with health services
and the Ministry of Health, which was renamed the Ministry of Health, Population
Control and Family Planning (MOHPP), was made responsible for the family
planning program. In 1975, a Population Planning Division was established
under the MOHPC for coordinating the population program and family planning
services were taken out of the Health Division and entrusted to the Popula-
tion Planning Division. Maternal and child health (MCH) services were soon
transferred to this Division, which was then renamed the Population Control
and Family Planning Division (PCFPD) (para. 2.20 and 2.21). Thus, integration
of MCH and family planning services was retained. However, inadequate co-
operation in the utilization of personnel and facilities by the two divisions
has created serious problems for delivery of MCH/FP services. Recently the
MOHPP has again been renamed as Ministry of Health and Population Control
(MOHPC).
2.08      Although PCFPD has recruited about 12,500 full-time female family
welfare assistants (FWAs) for field work, they are not yet working as teams
with family welfare workers (FWWs), their male counterparts in the Health
Division.  By 1978, only about 165 thana health complexes (THCs) and, more
seriously, only about 400 union-level family welfare centers (FWCs) were in
operation.
2.09      By 1976, the five ministries mentioned in the Plan, as well as
the MOHPC, were conducting population/family life education and information
programs. This multi-sectoral approach has been effective in drawing atten-
tion to population growth as a family, as well as a national, problem and in
creating a considerable demand for family planning services.
2.10      The GOB has encouraged private sector efforts.  An institute to
coordinate private sector activities was set up in 1978. A large contracep-
tive marketing project and a sterilization program conducted by two private
agencies have provided about one-fourth of the family planning services.



2.11      Although no legal measures or disincentives have been adopted, a
study of the fertility impact of national laws has been completed. Pilot
schemes designed to raise the socio-economic status of women have yielded
encouraging results in terms of increasing earning capacity and family plan-
ning acceptance and will be expanded during the second project period. The
National Population Council has been set up (para. 2.17).
2.12      In addition to implementation of the above strategies, the GOB has
been experimenting, during the past two years, with various types of schemes
to encourage community participation in the population sector program. Notable
among them is the success achieved by the self-reliant village scheme. In
about 9,000 villages, leaders have been trained to plan and implement important
development programs. Committees at village level are formed for different
types of sectoral development programs including population. An evaluation
of the scheme by the Bangladesh Institute of Development Studies in 20 sample
villages concluded that the scheme had a definite, positive impact on produc-
tion, mainly as a result of better irrigation facilities promoted by it. It
also made a positive contribution to the population control program: "The
village-level organizations facilitated discussions among local people and
government officials about the usefulness of family planning and its methods.
As a result, a large majority of heads of households came to approve family
planning and quite a significant proportion of them were currently practicing
family planning methods." In two intensive-input villages, 30% and 47% of
the respondents were practicing family planning (Annex 7, T-8).
2.13      On the other hand, the report points out that the level of people's
participation in the use of local resources and the benefits to poorer sec-
tions of the community were limited. Its replicability is not ensured since
the participants in the program relied heavily on Government resources. It
is important that these defects are remedied before the scheme is expanded
beyond a pilot phase.
Health
2.14      The First Five-Year Plan provided for a shift of emphasis from
curative to preventive health care and the delivery of both health and family
planning services in rural areas by teams of FWAs and FWWs with the support of
THCs and FWCs. In order to surmount the obstacle to the extension of rural
health services presented by the shortage of doctors, preventive and curative
services were to be entrusted as far as possible to paramedicals and auxil-
iaries; for this purpose, a new category of medical assistants (MAs) was to be
created. Health services staff were to be trained in community medicine, and
a system for data collection was to be organized.
2.15      Progress has not been as fast as was hoped.  As mentioned, the
construction and staffing of THCs has been slow. There has been negligible
progress towards the collection of health data. The eight THCs now being
constructed under the first project will provide field training facilities
for community medicine. Four training schools for MAs have been established.



- 8 -
2.16      The strategy to provide primary health care as envisaged in the
health policy under preparation now is to: (a) make village health workers
accountable to village health committees; (b) develop a referral system;
(c) provide one doctor for 5,000 population, one nurse per four beds and
one "village doctor" per village by 1985; (d) review and revise basic health
workers' functions; (e) control tuberculosis, leprosy and worm infestation;
and (f) extend rural water supply to cover all areas.
C. Organization
Policy Formulation and Coordination
2.17      National population policy is formulated by a National Population
Council consisting of representatives of the interested ministries and
agencies and chaired by the President. A Central Coordination Committee
comprising the secretaries of these ministries and presided over by the
Minister of Health and Population Control coordinates the multi-sectoral
information, education and motivation (IEM) program and service delivery
program. The GOB proposes to set up a National Health Council to advise on
health policy.
2.18      Coordinating committees have also been set up at district and thana
levels. Similar committees for unions and villages are being set up (Annex 7,
C-1).
Implementation
2.19      The MOHPC is responsible for coordination and implementation of the
health and population program. Other ministries and voluntary organizations
assist in the implementation of the program.
2.20      MOHPC.  The MOHPC is divided into two divisions--PCFPD and Health
Division. The PCFPD is responsible for the MCH and population program; the
Health Division is responsible for the remainder of health services. Both
divisions at headquarters level have ministerial wings call'ed the Secretar-
iats responsible for planning and monitoring, and operational wings called
the Directorates.
2.21      The PCFP Directorate has five functional units at headquarters.  It
has population control and family planning officers in district, subdivision
and thana levels for coordination and administration. At the union level,
the family planning assistant (FPA) is responsible for coordination. The
technical staff of the PCFP Directorate consists of district and thana medical
officers (MCH/FP), family welfare visitors (FWVs--paramedicals) at thana and
union levels, and family welfare assistants (FWAs--female multipurpose workers
at village level), (Annex 7, C-2).



- 9 -
2.22      The Health Division has two Directorates which administer preventive
and curative health services respectively. At the district level, a civil sur-
geon and a deputy civil surgeon are in charge of health and hospital services.
Below the district, the thana health administrator is the head of the thana
health complex (THC) and administers the entire health program in the thana.
At present, assistant health inspectors at the union level supervise FWWs.
However, the Health Division has started training medical assistants (MAs) who
will be posted at union level. FWCs will provide health care at the union
level and technical support to the FWWs who provide primary health care
at the village level (Annex 7, C-2).
2.23      Other Ministries and Agencies.  In addition to the MOHPC, the five
ministries mentioned in the Five-Year Plan (para. 2.06) serve as channels for
the dissemination of family planning information. Each of these ministries
has a population program officer appointed under the first project. Each
ministry has also strengthened its field staff to enable it to discharge its
population role. The coordination of the multi-sectoral program is achieved
through coordination committees as well as through regular meetings of popula-
tion program officers chaired by the PCFPD.
2.24      The Health and Population Planning Section of the Planning Commis-
sion reviews the budgetary allocations for the health and population programs
and is also responsible for the external evaluation of key program activities.
2.25      Voluntary Organizations.  The chief voluntary organizations partici-
pating in the health and family planning services are the Bangladesh Family
Planning Association, the Bangladesh Association for Voluntary Sterilization
and the Concerned Women for Family Planning. Others are the Christian Health
Care Project, the Companiganj Project, the People's Health Care Project and
the Family Planning Social Marketing Project. All these agencies and projects
are funded from external sources; a few of them also receive money from PCFPD.
PCFPD has a subvention committee which allocates funds to private organiza-
tions. A National Council of Voluntary Agencies was recently formed, and a
Family Planning Training and Service Center, financed by the Ford Foundation
and Family Planning International Assistance has been established to coordi-
nate and support private sector and voluntary efforts.
Management Study
2.26      A management study of the population program conducted as part of
the first project recommended that: (a) there should be a closer working
relationship between PCFPD and the Health Division; (b) the PCFPD's planning
(Secretariat) and operational wings (Directorate) should be merged; (c) the
PCFPD organization should be decentralized to the district level; (d) the
deputy commissioner at district level and the circle officer at thana level
should be involved in the administration of the family planning program;
(e) the sub-division should be developed as a supervisory tier between dis-
trict and thana; (f) union councils should help to supervise field workers;
(g) the FWV, FPA, FWA and Dai (midwife) at the union level should function as
a team; and (h) the Health Division should help in providing family planning
clinical services.



- 10 -
2.27      First, in accordance with recommendation (a) above, the Health
Division and PCFPD reached a working agreement for coordination and coopera-
tion of their activities in early 1978; furthermore, to resolve eventually
the problem of inadequate cooperation, the GOB proposes to integrate MCH/FP
with health services in well-defined phases (para. 2.47).  Second, the GOB
has recently approved a proposal for merging the PCFP Directorate and the
Division (see recommendation b). Third, the PCFPD has recently decentralized
its administration and has assigned definite functions to Deputy Commissioners
under this decentralized set up (see recommendations c and d, para. 2.48,
and Annex 7, C-3). Fourth, PCFPD has already developed a supervisory tier at
sub-division level--recommendation (e). Fifth, PCFPD is enlisting the help
of union councils in the supervision of field workers and the promotion of
family planning in villages--recommendation (f). Recommendation (g) is under
GOB's consideration; (h) will be implemented in the course of phased integra-
tion of MCH/FP with the health services.
D. Facilities
2.28      While PCFPD has its own manpower, it shares the Health Division's
physical facilties. Neither PCFPD nor Health Division has a full complement
of facilities and manpower.
2.29      While each of the 356 rural thanas, covering about 200,000 people,
should have a THC for curative and preventative health care, and for sterili-
zation and other family planning services, at present only 165 thanas have
a functioning THC. The number is expected to increase to 270 by July 1980.
Each THC comprises an outpatient clinic, a laboratory and a 31-bed hospital;
actual bed strength at present varies between 10 and 25. The full comple-
ment of THC staff consists of four medical officers, including a thana health
administrator, five staff nurses and supporting staff from the Health Divi-
sion, and one medical officer and two or three FWVs and supporting staff from
PCFPD. Because of the shortage of doctors, due to emigration and the reluc-
tance of doctors to join rural health services, only one or two of the sanc-
tioned four positions have been filled by the Health Division. PCFPD's
situation is worse, only nine of 356 positions were filled until recently,
when the Health Division assigned 100 doctors to PCFPD. Because of a general
shortage of nurses and emigration, a negligible number of staff nurses is
available for THCs. PCFPD has now trained enough FWVs to staff the THCs.
PCFPD inherited from the previous program a family planning clinic in each
thana. These clinics are being merged with the MCH/FP units at THCs. PCFPD
also has MCH centers at the district and sub-division levels.
2.30      Lack of physical facilities is more serious at the union level.
A FWC at the union level is expected to provide simple curative and preventive
health care and family planning services, with technical support from the
THC. The FWCs are the only source of institutional health care, referral
services and a base for the outreach program for about 20,000 population.
Only 400 of the 4,500 unions have FWCs, in rented inadequate premises. None
of these has a full complement of staff. The staff of a FWC consists of a MA,
a compounder and a health inspector from the Health Division, and a FWV and a



- 11 -
midwife from PCFPD. The FWWs, FWAs and FPAs use FWCs as their base station.
The first batch of MAs has just finished training and is undergoing intern-
ship. An adequate number of MAs and FWVs should be available by 1985.
2.31      Below the union level, no physical facilities exist.  The FWWS
from the Health Division and FWAs and FPAs from PCFPD deliver health and
family planning services at the village level. The FWWs are responsible for
malaria control, simple health care, referral of cases to health centers,
inoculation and health education, while FWAs and FPAs are responsible for the
delivery of contraceptives, the referral of family planning clients to clinics,
family planning motivation, and advice on MCH care. Since each worker has to
cover three or four villages, he/she can visit each household only once in
two or three months. This coverage is clearly inadequate. The situation is
made worse by inadequate technical support from the FWC or THC.
E. Financing
2.32      The annual budget of PCFPD increased from Tk 75 million (US$5
million) in 1974-75 to Tk 441 million (US$29.4 million) in 1978-79. The
allocation in 1978-79 constituted about 3% of the total annual development
budget, which compares favorably with 0.2% in Indonesia, 0.04% in Thailand
and 0.2% in the Philippines. The Health Division allocation increased from
Tk 196 million (US$13 million) in 1974-75 to Tk 542 million (US$36.1 million)
or about 3.7% of the annual development budget, in 1978-79.
2.33      Under the two-year plan for fiscal years 1978-80, the allocation
for health amounts to Tk 1,180 million (US$78 million) and for population
Tk 1,000 million (US$66 million).
2.34      About 80% of the PCFPD budget is allocated for rural services.
Although 60% of the Health Division's development budget is allocated for
rural health services, a large part of the revenue budget goes for hospitals
and research institutes. The entire PCFPD allocation comes out of the devel-
opment budget, and GOB has only recently funded a small part (US$1 million)
from the revenue budget of 1978-79.
2.35      A large part of the country's development budget is funded from
external sources. During the period 1973-78, total foreign assistance for
population control and family planning amounted to about US$74 million,
out of a total plan allocation of US$100 million. This included contributions
from Australia, Canada, Federal Republic of Germany, IDA, Norway, Sweden and
the United Kingdom (US$39.7 million), UNFPA (US$10 million) and USAID (US$14
million). In addition, Family Planning International Assistance, Ford Founda-
tion, Japan, Netherlands and Pathfinder Fund contributed about US$5 million to
the program.



- 12 -
F. Impact
2.36      The program has been able to create a large demand for family
planning services. While services delivery remains short of demand, some
progress in this respect is reported. Available service statistics show a
four-fold increase in sterilizations and a seven-fold increase in the distri-
bution of condoms and pills in 1977-78 compared with 1974-75 (see Annex 7,
T-9).  About 1.44 million married women are estimated, by the GOB, to have
been protected through these services. Also, the estimates indicate that over
77% of the services in 1978 were provided by the Government program with the
remaining coming from non-program sources (Annex 7, T-10). The BFS data on
contraceptive use indicate that about 8.2% of currently married women used
contraceptives in 1975. A survey carried out by the Institute of Statistical
Research and Training in 1978 suggests an increase in this proportion to about
16%. This increase in the contraceptive prevalence rate is also supported by
the tentative results of a number of ad hoc surveys. These figures have still
to be confirmed by a prevalence survey scheduled for late 1979. Although
actual contraceptive acceptance has closely matched the program targets set in
1973, it has fallen short of the revised targets set in 1976 (Annex 7, T-11).
2.37      Although detailed data on mortality and morbidity are not available,
serious diseases such as smallpox have been eradicated, and malaria was under
control until recently. Nevertheless, cholera and gastroenteritis remain
major causes of death and about 90% of children suffer from worm infestation.
To achieve even a small reduction in the incidence of disease will require a
considerable expansion of health facilities.
G. Program Assessment
2.38     The program, during the three years of its existence, has been
successful in setting up an organization from central to village level with
full-time staff, in training the paramedical manpower to expand the service
delivery, and in modest increases in the contraceptive prevalence rate. The
program and the socio-economic environment will need to be improved markedly
to achieve the present ambitious demographic targets.
Program Capacity
2.39      In order to attain the present target of fertility reduction, con-
traceptive usage would have to be extended to about 54% of currently married
women by 1990 (see Annex 7, T-12). This would reduce the crude birth rate
from 47 in 1975 to about 27 by 1990. Such a steep fall in the birth rate,
though desirable, may not be feasible. The contraceptive acceptance rate
required greatly exceeds the rates achieved in even the most successful
programs. While in a few developing countries, such as Singapore, Hong Kong
and Korea, the proportion of currently married women using contraceptives
reached 40% within 10-15 years of the start of the program, the decline of
fertility in these countries took place in an environment of fairly rapid
social and economic development (Annex 7, T-13). In contrast, Bangladesh
lacks the advantages of a fully established family planning program and a
strong economy.



- 13 -
2.40      A study rating the effectiveness of national family planning prog-
rams assigned Bangladesh a rating of 3 in 1975, compared with Singapore's 26,
Korea's 24 and India's 19. 1/ However, the progress the Bangladesh program
has made during the last two years (see para. 2.07-2.13) would probably
entitle it to a rating of 14. (See Annex 7, T-14 for details of rating.)
Even with the latter rating, the program can at best be considered moderate.
2.41      The same study also rates countries according to their nearness
to socio-economic "thresholds" favorable to a decline in fertility. On this
basis, Bangladesh is rated at 24, compared with Korea 81, Malaysia 71 and
Indonesia 45. Bangladesh at present seems to be a long way from these thres-
hold levels of development (see following table).
Variable                                      Threshold       Bangladesh in
Range /1        mid-1970s /2
Population in cities 20,000 +                       16-50%              8%
Non-agricultural labor force                        50-65%             23%
Life expectancy                                     60-70 years        48 years
Female marriage before age 20                       10-29%             76%
Female literacy                                     60-75%             12%
Hospital beds per 1,000 population                    5                0.2
Newspaper circulation per 1,000 population          70-100              4
/1   W. Parker Mauldin and Bernard Berelson, "Conditions of Fertility
Decline in Developing Countries, 1965-75," Studies in Family Planning,
the Population Council: Volume 9, No. 5, May 1978, pp. 90-128.
/2   Government of Bangladesh, Statistical Pocket Book of Bangladesh, 1978.
2.42      It is estimated by the GOB that about 25% of the eligible couples are
currently ready to accept family planning provided the services are delivered
properly. Thus, it appears that this level of acceptance can be achieved by
simply improving the delivery of services (see paras. 2.45-2.52). Increases
in the acceptance rate of this level have already been achieved in small areas
where family planning services are effectively delivered, such as in the
Matlab Thana and Christian Health Care Project areas. A further increase in
acceptance of family planning, of course, will depend on further program
improvement, community participation in health and population programs,
reduction in infant mortality, expansion of education particularly among women
and greater women's participation in economic activities. The non-formal
education and health care need to be expanded to a greater extent than has
been the case so far.



- 14 -
2.43      To reduce the death rate from 19 to 15 will also require a much
greater investment in health infrastructure than at present planned and a
rise in the standard of living which would improve nutrition and make people
more responsive to health care. The following table compares health facili-
ties in Bangladesh with other countries which have attained a low death rate
and Annex 7, T-13 shows the level of socio-economic development in these
countries.
Bangladesh     Malaysia      Korea      Singapore
Death Rate                  19             6.3           7          5.1
Population in 000
per physician             9.6           4.4           1.6         1.3
per nurse                62.7           0.9           1.7         0.4
Source: "Population and Family Planning Programs," Population Council,
1978.
Program Constraints
2.44      Major constraints of the program reflected in the inadequate
delivery of family planning and health services are:
2.45      Facilities.  Only about 15% of the population is reached by insti-
tutional health and family planning services. Even the existing facilities
do not have a full complement of staff. Although about 12,500 FWAs have
been posted, their performance is poor due to inadequate on-the-job training
and technical supervision both from field officers and headquarters staff.
Training of supervisors is non-existent and transport is not available for
village visits. The fieldworkers are, therefore, left alone without logistics
or technical support. Even where staff and infrastructure exist, shortages
of drugs and other supplies make it difficult to deliver effective health and
family planning services. The second project is being designed to remove
these constraints.
2.46      Organization.  Inadequate cooperation between the Health and PCFP
Divisions and lack of technical and administrative support and supervision
of PCFPD field staff are the main organizational bottlenecks. A working
agreement between PCFPD and the Health Division reached in 1978 provided
inter alia for the transfer by the Health Division of medical officers (MCH/
FP) to PCFPD, but this transfer has run into considerable difficulties within
the medical profession.



- 1 5 -
2.47      Due to this continuing problem of inadequate cooperation between
the Health Division and PCFPD, the GOB has decided to integrate MCH/FP ser-
vices with health services. This integration will imply transferring the
responsibility for delivering MCH/FP services to the Health Division. A draft
plan of action for integration of MCH/FP services with health services at
the thana level and below has been prepared and approved by the GOB. The
plan states that integration will be most feasible and least disruptive if
phased out over a period of five to ten years. The transfer of MCH/FP services
to the Health Division will start immediately (July 1, 1979) at 20 district
and 45 sub-divisional hospitals. At thana level and below the transfer of
these services will be linked to the establishment of MCH/FP units at the
existing THCs and the construction and operation of the proposed FWCs. Thus
the transfer of services at the thana level and below, according to the
present schedule, will commence on July 1, 1983 with the completion of the
establishment of 700 FWCs during the project period. Such phasing will
allow evaluation of performance in areas where services are integrated before
proceeding further. During negotiations agreement was reached that the
GOB shall implement the agreed plan of phased integration of MCH/FP services
with health services and shall review its progress at regular intervals in
consultation with the Association. Meanwhile, as a step towards achieving the
overall objective of strengthening MCH/FP services, the Health Division should
continue to depute doctors to PCFPD for filling its medical officer positions.
2.48      The PCFPD has started to improve field operations by decentralizing
its administrative structure to the district level. It has begun appointing
additional technical staff and officers at the level of Deputy Director
responsible for the population program in the districts (Annex 7, C-3). The
Deputy Commissioners will be responsible for approving the travel of Deputy
Directors, as well as their confidential performance report and leave sanctions.
At the headquarters, the PCFP Directorate will be merged with the Division
and four Directors each responsible for the program in five districts will
be designated.
2.49      Despite the above proposals, the inadequate cooperation between
the PCFPD and the Health Division may still persist. Furthermore, frequent
reorganizations in the past (para. 2.07), have contributed to slow implemen-
tation of the program. Even an ideal organizational structure will not be
meaingful unless program implementation improves. During negotiations,
the GOB agreed to maintain the district and national-level organizations being
set up and, in the event of any significant changes in these organizations,
consult the Association and take into account the Association's comments, if
any.
2.50      Administration.  Although administrative problems are common to
all development activities in Bangladesh, the critical priority of population
and health requires that special attention be given to the early resolution
of: (a) the shortage of highly skilled manpower, such as doctors, nurses,
managers, architects and engineers due to emigration; (b) cumbersome proce-
dures for the release and use of funds; (c) slow decision-making; (d) absence



- 16 -
of incentives to improve staff performance; and (e) delays in recruitment of
staff and advisers. Agreement was reached, during negotiations, that the GOB
will review the PCFPDs' administrative, financial and recruitment procedures
for direct recruitment of staff and advisers to allow speedy implementation of
the population program and make appropriate modifications as are
feasible to these procedures by December 31, 1979.
2.51      Strategy.  Although the experiments in community participation ild
both health and population programs have been encouraging (para. 2.12), the
GOB has no definite strategy or plan for promoting community participation.
Agreement was reached, during negotiations, that the GOB shall review existing
programs of community participation in health and population activities,
particularly population activities in village self-reliant programs by
March 31, 1980, and on the basis of such review prepare in consultation
with the Association a detailed strategy to strengthen these activities
by September 30, 1980.
Requirements
2.52      Major program requirements for 1978-85 as identified by GOB 1/ and
WHO 2/ are summarized below:
1/   Government of Bangladesh, PCFPD.  The Two-year (1978-80) and Second Five
year (1980-85) Plans. Dacca, September, 1977.
2/   WHO - Country Health Programming in Bangladesh--Programme Proposal,
August 1977, Dacca.
