2.53          The  investment  for meeting  the major  requirements  alone  amounts  to
USSl.4 billion, which the country with its present shortages of skilled man-
puwer and materials cannot absorb during the next seven years. The program,
and therefore its impact, will have to be phased over a longer period. The
proposed second project meets only eight percent of the requirement and is
therefore only one small step towards achieving the population and health
program goals. In addition to the proposed second project, about US$100
million are expected from other funding agencies, particularly UNFPA (US$50
million) and USAID (US$40-50 million) during 1980-85. The total external
assistance, according to present indications during the next seven years,
will amount to about US$220 million. Expected inputs from various sources
are summarized in Annex 1. About US$71 million of estimated annual recurring
cost for health and US$77 million for population, as shown in the above table,
will require a doubling of the present revenue budget allocation for health
and the total budget allocation for PCFPD by 1985 (Annex 7, T-15).



- 18 -
III. THE FIRST POPULATION PROJECT
Objective
3.01      The object of the first project was to increase the demand for and
supply of MCH and family planning services. Toward this end, the project
assisted the GOB to: (a) train more MCH and family planning paramedical
workers; (b) increase the number of MCH/FP field workers; (c) establish pilot
schemes for introducing family life education into the activities of five
sectoral ministries and for women's vocational training; (d) strengthen the
capacity of the mass media to deal with population topics; (e) build up
research and evaluation capacities; and (f) support private sector activities
and innovative ideas.
Project Description
3.02      The project included:  (a) constructing, furnishing and equipping:
(i) one College of Nursing for training nurse tutors; (ii) eight FWV training
schools; (iii) eight THCs and 24 union FWCs for the field training of medical
and paramedical personnel; and (iv) four model family planning clinics
attached to medical colleges; (b) salary support for 3,700 FWAs to improve the
delivery of MCH and family planning services in rural areas; and (c) equipment,
vehicles, technical assistance and operating cost (salaries, material and
travel allowance) to: (i) carry out pilot schemes in 61 thanas designed
to incorporate family life education in the activities of the Ministries of
Agriculture; Education; Information and Broadcasting; Local Government, Rural
DeveloDment and Cooperatives; Labor and Social Welfare; and MOHPC; (ii) prepare
population education curricula; (iii) establish a population program cell in
Radio Bangladesh, strengthen the film development laboratory, and expand other
mass media activities; (iv) establish a Service Statistics Unit in PCFPD and
an External Evaluation Unit (EEU) in the Planning Commission; and (v) enable
the Bangladesh Institute of Development Studies to conduct a study of the
determinants of reproductive behavior. The project also included a study of
the impact of national laws on fertility, a management study of the population
program, the engagement of architects to design and supervise construction,
and funds for support to private sector and innovative activities.
Financing
3.03      The total cost of the project amounted to US$45.7 million.  This
cost is being financed by IDA (US$15 million), NORAD (NKr 45 million--US$8.5
million), Federal Republic of Germany (DM 15 million--US$6.1 million),
Australia (A$2.0 million--US$2.6 million), UK - ODM (E1.4 million--US$3.2
million), CIDA (CN$2.0 million--US$2.0 million), SIDA (Skr 15 million--US$3.0
million), and GOB (US$5.3 million equivalent). The total amount available
from the six donors was US$25.4 million equivalent at the exchange rate pre-
vailing in September 1975.
3.04      The devaluation of the taka in 1977, combined with a lower-than-
estimated rate of increase in costs and delays in initiation of various
schemes, resulted in savings of about US$18.0 million. With the agreement



- 19 -
of the co-financiers of the project, about US$15.6 million of the savings are
being used to expand crucial project activities which, inter alia include:
(a) extending salary support for 8,000 FWAs through June 1980; (b) training
union council chairmen and village leaders to promote family planning;
(c) training program managers; (d) extending the IEM pilot schemes until June
1980; (e) providing 19 teams for retraining FWAs; (f) purchasing additional
films; and (g) constructing 80 union FWCs through community participation on
a pilot basis to test the feasibility of this method for construction on a
larger scale during the second project. The remaining amount is reserved for
contingencies.
Implementation Mechanism
3.05      The primary responsibility for implementing the project rests with
PCFPD in collaboration with the Health Division and five other ministries.
The only new units established under the project have been: (a) the Building
Planning and Design Unit (BPDU) under the Health Division, which monitors and
supervises construction; (b) the Project Finance Cell under PCFPD for keeping
accounts and submitting withdrawal applications; and (c) population program
officers in the five ministries mentioned in para. 3.02.
Progress
3.06      The buildings on 45 sites originally included in the project are being
constructed under 21 contracts, 19 of which have been awarded after consider-
able delay due to problems in acquiring sites and time-consuming contracting
procedures. Tenders for one further contract have been received leaving only
one FWV school on which action has still to be taken. All sites have now
been acquired. It is estimated that all construction will be completed by
the last quarter of 1980--one year behind the appraisal schedule and six months
behind the master plan schedule. Progress on 80 pilot FWCs has been satisfac-
tory. Seventy-seven of the 80 FWCs are under construction and 50 of these
have reached roofing and finishing levels within the last 12 months. The
procurement of equipment, vehicles, technical assistance and the appointment
of additional staff is nearing completion (Annex 2).
Activities
3.07      During the three-year implementation period since September 1975,
the first project has helped the GOB to set up an organization capable of
implementing a broad-based multi-sectoral population program; to review this
organization on the basis of a management study (para. 2.26); to set up a
service statistics and program evaluation system; to increase the number of
MCH/FP field workers; to increase the capacity of FWV training schools and
the capacity of the College of Nursing to train tutors; to establish pilot
women's development schemes; to extend and strengthen mass media activities;
to prepare population education curricula; to initate a study of the deter-
minants of fertility and of the impact of national laws on fertility; to
initiate the community participation in the population program through self-
reliant village programs; and to test the feasibility of constructing FWCs
through community participation (Annex 2). Preliminary evaluation of all major



- 20 -
project components and activities such as field workers' performance, training
of FWVs and FWAs, Radio Bangladesh's family planning program, women's develop-
ment schemes, service statistics system and the self-reliant village program
has been completed. Results of this evaluation are summarized in paras. 4.06,
4.27, 4.33, 4.39, 4.47-4.48, 4.63 and 4.73. Related documents are listed in
Annex 8.
Impact
3.08      It appears that the project has succeeded in creating demand for
family planning services through its motivational program and in modest
increases in the contraceptive prevalence rate (para. 2.36). It has also
contributed to an increase in the number of trained paramedicals.
3.09      In the absence of reliable data, it is difficult to assess the
demographic impact of the program and to separate the impact of the project
from the program as a whole. However, with available information, from
various evaluation studies, on acceptors recruited through different program
components, an attempt is made to assess the achievement of program and
project targets as set for the first project period (1975-78).  nata are
given in Annex 7, T-16.  It is estimated that about 1.3 million acceptors
would have been recruited through the program as against the target of 0.9
million. The reasons for achievement exceeding the target were: appointment
of a larger number of FWAs than expected; low targets set at the time of
formulation of the first project because of the difficulties that were anti-
cipated and the availability of ready clients. The impact of the acceptance
level on birth rate cannot be determined as contraceptive continuation rates
are not known.
Expenditures and Disbursements
3.10      After initial delays, particularly in respect of construction and
vehicle and equipment procurement, progress is now satisfactory and $31.6
million, or 80% of the combined external financing made available, have been
committed. About US$13.1 million or 42% of the amount committed has been
disbursed (Annex 7, T-17). Disbursements have been below target but, with
construction expenditures now accelerating and special measures to expedite
the processing of project expenditures and reimbursement requests under way,
the IDA credit and the bilateral grants are expected to be substantially
disbursed by the Closing Date, currently December 31, 1980 (Annex 7, T-18).
Delay and Problems in First Project Implementation
3.11      The construction of buildings and the procurement of equipment
and vehicles have been delayed by about one year. The main reasons for
delay are: (a) delay in organizing and staffing the Population Planning
Division of MOHPP, which was a condition of credit effectiveness; (b) delay
in appointing an architectural consultant and weak staffing and inadequate
facilities of BPDU; (c) difficulty in acquiring sites. Remedial measures
to overcome these problems during the second project period have been recom-
mended in paras. 5.15-5.30.



- 21 -
IV. THE SECOND PROJECT
A. Object and Design
4.01      Object.  The object of the project is to reduce fertility and
mortality by: (a) primarily extending and strengthening the family planning
service and health care delivery system; (b) increasing and improving the
quality of training of health and family planning manpower; (c) extending
the IEM activities of the first project; (d) strengthening evaluation and
research capacities; and (e) improving program and project implementation.
4.02      Design.  The project is designed to continue and expand the activi-
ties begun under the first project. It is based on the population and health
program strategies described in paras. 2.06 and 2.14, and takes into account
GOB's investment priorities and implementation capacity (paras. 2.44-2.53),
and the experience gained under the first project (paras. 3.07 and 3.11).
B. Composition
4.03      The project includes:
(a)  Health Care and Family Planning Service Delivery
(i)  strengthening the delivery of health and family
planning services at the village level by,
inter alia, providing continuing salary support
for the FWAs recruited under the first project, 11
(6,500 in the second year, 5,500 in the third, and
4,500 in the fourth year, providing 253 ambulances
for FWVs, four vehicle maintenance vans, 19 vehicles
for supervision from the district, and providing
FWAs with two-way radios and 4,000 bicycles on a
pilot basis;
(ii)  improving health, MCH and family planning ser-
vices by constructing, furnishing and equipping:
(a) 700 new FWCs; and (b) 19 THCs; and
(iii)  improving health, MCH and family planning services
by providing drugs.
1/   Savings from the first project were used to provide salary support for
8,000 FWAs from January 1979 and this support will continue through
the initial year of the second project, terminating in June 1980.



- 22 -
(b) Training of Health and Family Planning Manpower
(i) continuing the MCH/FP training of medical students
by continuing operating cost 1/ support for the four
model family clinics provided under the first project;
(ii) improving the training of FWVs by providing, inter
alia, technical assistance, books, materials and
operating expenses for refresher courses, and ex-
panding an existing FWV training school to accommodate
about 80 students;
(iii) expanding the training capacity of MAs by construct-
ing, equipping and furnishing and providing vehicles,
fellowships and students' stipends for four new
training schools for MAs;
(iv) strengthening the training of FWAs and FPAs by con-
structing, equipping and furnishing, and providing
advisory assistance and operating expenses for
19 training centers for FWAs and FPAs; and
(v) improving the training of PCFPD staff in management
by constructing, equipping and furnishing the training
building for the PCFPD.
(c) Information, Education and Motivation Activities
improving the dissemination of information on family
planning and health through: (a) mass media activi-
ties of the Ministry of Information and Broadcasting
by providing, inter alia, vehicles, equipment, fellow-
ships and continuing and incremental operating costs;
(b) women's development programs in about 84 thanas
(42 of first project and 42 new) by providing, inter
alia, equipment, vehicles, technical assistance, con-
tinuing (except mothers' clubs) and incremental operat-
ing costs; construction, equipment and furniture for
women's vocational training centers in 12 thanas and
by establishing a marketing and design cell; (c) the
training of community leaders and use of folk media by
the IEM Unit of PCFPD by providing incremental operat-
ing costs; and (d) strengthening the Health Education
Bureau of the Health Division by providing, inter alia,
equipment, vehicles, technical assistance, and incre-
mental operating costs.
1/   Operating cost includes salaries, vehicle maintenance, materials and
travel allowances. Continuing operating cost refers to continuation of
operating cost support initiated in the first project and incremental
cost refers to operating cost of new activities under the second project.



- 23 -
(d) Evaluation and Research
(i) increasing evaluation and monitoring capacity
by providing vehicles, equipment, technical
assistance and continuing and incremental oper-
ating costs of the Service Statistics Unit of
PCFPD and the External and Evaluation Unit of
the Planning Commission; and
(ii) assisting the Health Information Unit of the
Health Division to collect health management
information and establish medical audit teams
by providing vehicles, equipment, technical
assistance and incremental operating cost.
(e) Innovative and Private Sector Activities. making
funds available for financing innovative population
subprojects in the public and private sectors.
(f) Project Implementation. strengthening project
implementation capacity by: (a) providing the BPDU,
the FWC Administrative Unit, the Supplies Direc-
torate, the Public Works Department and the
Project Finance Cell of PCFPD with vehicles,
equipment, consultant services, and incremental
operating cost; (b) providing additional staff for
the Population Program Officers at four implementing
Ministries other than MOHPC; and (c) providing
additional staff to assist the Population Project
Officer at the Bank's Dacca office.
C. Description
Health and Family Planning Service Delivery
4.04      The recent WHO country health program paper states that Bangladesh's
health system, with which family planning services are inextricably linked,
should be improved by providing: (a) better primary health care through
home visits and community action at the village level; (b) better out-patient
and in-patient care through the completion and staffing of THCs and FWCs,
which will also be responsible for supervising the village health workers;
(c) stronger communicable disease control programs; (d) better water supply
and sanitation; (e) better training facilities for health and family plan-
ning workers; (f) more efficient procurement and distribution of supplies
and equipment; and (g) an increased supply of drugs. The project includes
support for (a), (b), (e) and (g); UNICEF is supporting (c) and (d), and
UNFPA (f).



- 24 -
4.05      Basic Health and Family Planning Field Workers.  At the village
level, 12,000 FWWs (Health Division) and 12,500 FWAs (PCFPD) deliver primary
health care and family planning services through home visits. (Annex 3 sum-
marizes their functions.) Unlike the FWWs, the FWAs have been added only
recently, and their salaries have so far been provided from external resources.
The salaries of 8,000 FWAs are being financed under the first project from
January 1978 until June 1980. UNFPA has been supporting another 1,800 FWAs
and is expected to continue this support. In view of the vital importance
of FWAs, the second project will continue salary support for the FWAs for
three years, starting in July 1980, on a declining basis as described in
para. 4.03(a). The GOB will finance the salaries of workers not covered by
the project or UNFPA. Details of financing are given in Annex 7, T-19 and 20.
4.06      Not surprisingly, considering the speed at which they were recruited,
the performance of FWAs in terms of household visits, recruitment of acceptors,
treatment of complications arising out of contraceptive use and MCH care has
so far been below standard. The main reasons for poor performance have been
identified by PCFPD 1/ as: (a) inadequate basic training; (b) absence of tech-
nical supervision and on-the-job training; (c) lack of training of supervisors
in supervision; (d) absence of drugs and logistics support such as transport;
and (e) absence of adequate community support for contraceptive use. Further-
more, about 40% of the workers were found to be related to influential members
of the community which make administrative control difficult. Since FWAs and
FPAs are the only sources of MCH/FP services at the community level, improvement
in their performance is critical for the program.
4.07      A Plan of Action for improving the performance of FWAs and FPAs has
been agreed during negotiations. First, according to this plan, a retraining
for FWAs and training in supervision for their supervisors including FWVs,
Population Control and Family Planning Officers and FPAs are being organized
(paras. 4.34 and 4.36). Second, while the FPAs will provide logistic support
to FWAs, the FWVs will be providing technical support and on-the-job training.
For this purpose, additional FWVs are being posted at THCs pending the estab-
lishment of FWCs which will then become the base of outreach programs. The
FWVs will combine on-the-job training with delivery of community level MCH
and family planning services. The FWVs will also stay for a week with FWAs
requiring special attention and will give them intensive on-the-job training.
This training should help to give the FWA's confidence in carrying out their
work and create credibility with the clients. Furthermore, definite instruc-
tions for headquarters staff to visit the field at least 10 days a month will
be issued.  Third, the GOB will provide FWAs and FPAs with limited supplies of
medicine, nutrition supplements and first-aid kits to provide basic medical
care and enhance their acceptability within the community. Fourth, the social
sanction to contraception is being sought by using union and village health
and family planning committees and mothers' clubs and women's cooperatives
1/   PCFPD--A study on the Performance and Training of Field Workers
(FWAs and FPAs), December 1978. This was a follow-up study of another
evaluation conducted by the Planning Commission in November 1977.



- 25 -
to promote the FWA's work. Fifth, union and village committees are being made
responsible for monitoring FWAs and giving them support. Sixth, a system of
incentives and disincentives related to performance will be worked out.
Seventh, a change in the methodology of work of FWAs is being contemplated by
the GOB. Recognizing that individuals differ in their responsiveness to the
program in the initial stages, the FWAs will devote the necessary time and
attention to prospective clients, based on individual needs. Careful attention
will be paid to individuals expressing readiness to accept contraception and
they will be contacted regularly. The satisfied acceptor will then be used to
diffuse family planning and MCH care information within the neighborhood and
beyond. Each FWA will be assigned a geographic area which she can conveniently
cover. Some uncovered areas will be assigned to part-time workers. Eighth, a
two-way radio system will be used to improve the communication link between
FWAs and THCs. Ninth, the FWWs of the Health Division will work closely with
FWAs. This cooperation will result in combining family planning with total
health care at the village level. A schedule for the above actions ig given
in Annex 4. Agreement was reached, during negotiations, that the GOB shall:
(a) carry out this agreed plan of action by June 30, 1980; (b) furnish required
information on implementation of the plan to the Association by December 31,
1980; and (c) commencing July 1 each year evaluate the activities and perform-
ance of FWAs and FPAs in consultation with the Association and furnish to the
Association by October 31 each year reports of such evaluation, and based on
the results of the reports, take corrective actions as may be satisfactory to
the Association.
4.08      To facilitate the above actions, the project provides transport and
communication facilities which are seriously lacking (4.03(a) and Annex 7,
T-20). At present, neither FWVs nor THCs have transport to supervise field
work or carry patients, even in emergencies. About 17 four-wheel drive
vehicles (ambulances) are being procured for FWVs, using first project savings.
An additional 253 such vehicles will be provided under the second project, so
that each of the 270 THCs which are expected to be functioning by July 1981
will have one. These vehicles, in addition to transporting patients, will
enable FWVs to organize mobile clinics in villages, where they will also give
FWAs on-the-job training and technical support. Four mobile vans will be
provided for preventive maintenance of these vehicles. In addition, each one
of the PCFPD district offices will have one vehicle for supervision of field
work. During negotiations, the GOB agreed to: (a) take all actions necessary
to maintain the vehicles and equipment for the project in good working con-
dition; and (b) in consultation with the Association, undertake an analysis of
its system for maintenance and use of such vehicles and equipment by December
31, 1979 and shall thereafter take steps as may be necessary to strengthen the
system.
4.09      A survey undertaken by PCFPD indicates that about 25% of the FWAs
are willing to use bicycles. The project provides bicycles for about 50% of
the FWAs supported through the project. It is possible that, as some FWAs
begin using bicycles, others may follow suit. All 18,000 FWAs and FPAs will
be given first-aid kits and about 2,000 kits will be kept in reserve.



- 26 -
4.10      FWAs at present cannot commute to THCs conveniently because of lack
of transport and the long distances between villages and THCs. During the
monsoons, some villages are isolated. FWAs and FPAs must walk 10-20 miles
to schedule sterilizations. If the trial in three thanas proves the proposed
radio system (citizens band) effective, it would enable FWAs to obtain instruc-
tions in complicated cases, call for ambulances and schedule sterilizations.
In a few unions of the pilot thanas where there are FWCs, the system will
also be used for communication between FWAs and FWCs and between FWCs and
the THCs. Workers of other ministries will also have access to these radios.
The system should be simple to maintain, as the batteries will be charged by
solar energy and one technician in each thana will be trained to maintain
them. The field workers will be trained to use them. A common vocabulary
will be developed to avoid mistakes in communication, and PCFPD will monitor
and evaluate the system.
4.11      If the performance of the FWAs improves, the number of women and
children served through MCH services will be about 6.5 million, or about
10% of the women and children requiring these services. At a minimum rate
of 50 acceptors per year, workers supported through the project could recruit
about 675,000 acceptors per year.
4.12      The GOB will evaluate the impact of the above inputs on field
workers' performance. The FWAs supported through the project will be posted
in the 270 thanas where THCs are expected to be functioning by July 1981, thus
enabling their performance to be evaluated under the project. An illustrative
list of criteria for evaluation and a schedule are given in Annexes 4 and 5.
4.13      Family Welfare Centers (FWCs).  Since only 400 FWCs exist at pre-
sent, there is neither provision for outpatient care in 90% of the unions
nor any technical support for field workers. A survey of available physical
facilities conducted by the PCFPD showed that, about 665 of the 920 existing
Health Division dispensaries could be used as FWCs, provided that some minor
repairs and renovations were carried out. These 665 dispensaries, together
with about 400 FWCs, will permit the establishment of 1,065 FWCs by 1979.
More than 2,600 additional FWCs will be required to cover all 3,698 rural
unions by 1985.
4.14      The rate at which FWCs can be constructed and brought into operation
is limited by:  (a) the system of construction; and (b) the output of MAs to
staff them. FWCs provided under the project will be constructed by the experi-
mental method being used for the 80 FWCs financed from first project savings.
Under this method, construction, instead of being carried out by the Public
Works Department is entrusted to the local community through the medium
of Construction Committees headed by union council chairmen. The progress
of the 80 FWCs has demonstrated the technical feasibility of this method of
construction. However, because of the need for close supervision and the
scarcity of architects and engineers, it is estimated that not more than
300-400 FWCs can be constructed annually. The number of FWCs that can be
built during the four-year project period is, therefore, conservatively esti-
mated at about 1,200. The GOB sought assistance from other external sources
for about 500 FWCs, and the second project provides for the construction of
and equipment for another 700 (details in Annex 7, T-21).



- 27 -
4.15      The FWCs will be constructed and operated initially by PCFPD before
their eventual transfer to the Health Division. During negotiations, the GOB
agreed to prepare or cause to be prepared by December 31, 1979 an operational
manual for maintaining and operating FWCs.
4.16      Because of the unique system of construction and the large number
of FWCs, construction will be divided into three phases--230 in the first
phase and 235 in each of the second and third phases. Any shortfall in
construction during the first three years will be made good during the fourth.
The phasing of construction will be confirmed when the master plan for con-
struction currently under preparation by the architectural consultant is
completed. During negotiations, the GOB agreed to undertake the construction
of FWCs in accordance with the master plan; have progress and quality of such
construction evaluated annually by the architectural consultants; and undertake
no further construction under the project unless such progress and quality
are satisfactory to the Association. The impact of FWCs on health and
family planning services will be evaluated one year after the FWCs are put in
operation in a union. The construction of FWCS will be evaluated by the
architectural/ engineering consultants for the Droject and operations by the Planning
Commission. The proposed schedule is given in Annex 4 .
4.17      The 700 FWCs will be located in unions with access to a THC and
where no existing building is available. The distribution of FWCs by dis-
tricts is based on rural population. Each FWC will provide about 20,000
people with primary health care and family planning services. These FWCs
are expected to serve 10.5 million women and children with health care and
133,000 sterilization clients during the project period. They will improve
the performance of field workers by providing them with technical support.
4.18      Thana Health Complexes (THCs).  Since large thanas need two THCs,
about 368 THCs will be required for the 356 rural thanas. Excluding the
165 existing THCs, 203 (additional) THCs are required. Buildings are under
construction in 154, while in about 30 some construction has been started.
The project includes construction, equipment and furniture for the remaining
19 THCs. Each of the 19 thanas will cover a population of 200,000 by out-
patient and hospital services. These THCs will also strengthen the services
at FWCs through supervision and referral.
4.19      The MCH/FP services are often neglected in THCs.  At present, no
THC staff can be spared exclusively for MCH services. The establishment of
a separate MCH/FP unit is therefore essential. The available physical facil-
ities of the THCs will be used for this purpose. Each MCH/FP unit would be
headed by a medical officer assisted by two FWVs. Not all THCs have FWVs,
as some of them are still posted at separate family planning clinics. PCFPD
proposes to merge these clinics with the MCH/FP units. Agreement was reached,
during negotiations, that MCH/FP units will be established at the 270 THCs to
be in operation by July 31, 1981. These MCH/FP units will help, not only to
reduce infant and maternal mortality, but also to improve family planning
program performance. They are expected to perform at least 135,000 sterili-
zations (male and female) and IUD insertions annually. An estimated ten
million women and children are expected to receive health care through these
units.



- 28 -
4.20      Drugs.  Fieldworkers are not provided with drugs, and even the FWVs
at thana level have limited supplies. This shortage is partly due to logis-
tical problems, but to a much greater extent to the inadequate budget provi-
sion for drugs. UNICEF has been supplying drug and dietary supplement kits.
UNICEF will be able to provide only half the present estimated annual require-
ments of 30,000 kits at present, rising to 44,000 by 1983. Furthermore, the
rapid expansion of primary health care will increase the demand for drugs. To
overcome the shortage of drugs, GOB proposes to expand the present MOHPC drug
factory.
4.21      An interim report, by the consultants appointed by the GOB, on the
relative merits of expanding the MOHPC factory and buying drugs from commercial
pharmaceutical firms not only endorses the GOB proposal but also recommends
the establishment of four more MOHPC factories in order to meet the entire
needs of the public health system by 1985. The report also estimates that
these drugs would be 27% to 75% cheaper than commercially produced drugs. A
final judgment on this issue can only be reached in the light of the final
recommendations of the feasibility study. An increase of MOHPC capacity would
in any case take three years to complete. About US$4 million for the purchase
of basic drugs, is, therefore, included in the project (Annex 7, T-22). These
drugs could be procured in the form of drug and dietary supplement kits through
UNICEF. The use and suitability of drugs will be evaluated regularly by
PCFPD. The UNPFA will strengthen the logistics system for distribution of
drugs.
4.22      Evaluation.  In all, the project will strengthen health care and
family planning services in the 270 thanas by providing field workers, drugs,
vehicles and MCH/FP units. One hundred of these 270 thanas will also have
fully staffed and equipped FWCs. These 100 thanas will be evaluated to
measure the impact of the combined inputs on fertility and mortality, and
the results will be compared with the other 170 thanas.
Training of Health and Family Planning Manpower
4.23      Rural health and family planning services are delivered by doctors
and paramedicals such as FWVs and MAs at the union level, and FWWs, FWAs and
FPAs at the village level. Only a small number of nurses are engaged in rural
health services. Training capacity for medical and paramedical manpower, with
the additions under the second project, will be adequate to meet requirements
at least up to 1985. However, the shortages of medical personnel and nurses
are likely to persist unless the GOB can limit their emigration and make them
available for rural health services. The training of paramedicals, as evalua-
tions indicate, is weak, and these personnel are therefore unable to meet the
health and family planning needs of the population adequately.
4.24      The GOB proposes to make a study of:  (a) the long-term manpower
needs of the health and population program; (b) the extent to which the cur-
rent training prepares workers for their functions; and (c) the consequent
training requirements. Such a functional analysis should enable the GOB to
formulate a health manpower policy covering the next 25 years and to develop
a national training plan for 1980-85. Agreement was reached, during negotia-
tions, that the GOB shall: (a) in consultation with the Association, undertake
a functional analysis of its health and population programs for determination



- 29 -
of the manpower requirements at thana level and below by December 31, 1979;
and (b) prepare a health and population manpower policy, including a national
training program by December 31, 1980.
4.25      Doctors.  The medical colleges have lacked facilities for training
doctors in community medicine, new contraceptive technology and for clinical
contraceptive research. The first project financed the construction and
equipping of eight field THCs for training in community medicine, and a model
family planning clinic for contraceptive training and research at each of the
four medical colleges. USAID is funding model family planning clinics in the
other four medical colleges. These buildings (THCs and model clinics) are
expected to be completed by December 1980. The second project will continue
operating cost support for the four model family planning clinics for three
years. (For estimates, see Annex 7, T-23.) It is expected that the GOB will
be able to take over the cost of operating these clinics at the end of the
project period. It is proposed that these clinics should also be used for
the training of medical students in MCH care. Each clinic has 25 beds for
sterilization.
4.26      Nurses' Training.  The project does not include nurses' training,
as about nine schools each with a capacity of 100 students are under con-
struction through GOB's own resources. These should be adequate to meet
the requirements of hospital and health services at least up to 1990.
Furthermore, the shortage of nurses can also be reduced by using FWVs (of
whom there will be an adequate supply) as assistant nurses.
4.27      Family Welfare Visitors (FWVs) Training.  The FWVs are the key para-
medical personnel for delivering MCH/FP services in rural areas. The first
project included the construction of eight FWV schools. Pending the comple-
tion of the buildings, UNFPA helped to establish the schools in temporary
buildings. In all, 11 FWV schools have been established so far. The supply
of FWVs from existing schools will meet the requirements up to 1985 and beyond
(Annex 7, T-24). The quality of training in these schools, however, as indi-
cated by a Planning Commission evaluation, is impaired by inadequate provision
of tutors, tutor training, libraries, field training and training manuals.
4.28      As recommended by the Planning Commission, PCFPD has already taken
action to: (a) fill the vacant staff positions at FWV training schools and
assigned sister tutors as principals where feasible; (b) increase the supply
of textbooks and trainees' manuals; and (c) increase the practical content
of FWV training. The shortage of tutors is being eased by reviving the tutor
training program at the College of Nursing. In the meantime, the GOB has
entered into a contract with the voluntary Christian Health Care Project to
provide tutors and field training. The training of tutors will be improved
through workshops to be organized by the GOB, using first project funds.
The second project will provide: (a) the operating cost (travel allowances,
stipends and training materials) of retraining FWVs; (b) technical assistance
and materials for preparing training manuals and teaching aids and for the
training of tutors; and (c) books and publications. It will also provide
additional classrooms, dormitories, libraries and staff quarters to enable
the FWV training school at Barisal to accommodate 80 instead of 30 students
(Annex 7, T-25).



- 30 -
4.29      The second project will help to make good the deficiencies in the
basic training of FWVs. The existing 1,800 FWVs will be given two weeks'
retraining at the FWV schools. The retraining program is expected to be
completed by 1982. Tutors will require annual refresher training at the
College of Nursing. PCFPD will, with the assistance of an adviser provided
in the project, review the curriculum for FWVs and prepare manuals for tutors,
trainees and FWVs.
4.30      Medical Assistants (MAs).  To relieve the shortage of doctors in
rural areas, the GOB started to train MAs--a new category of paramedical
staff--in 1976. The course consists of two years of basic training and six
months of internship. Like FWVs, MAs will be assigned primarily to union
FWCs, where they will provide curative and preventive health services.
4.31      The GOB, with support from the Netherlands, has established four
training schools for MAs, each with an annual intake of 100 students. ,The
first group of 330 students (excluding dropouts) completed their basic
training in December 1978. These four schools would produce about 2,500 MAs
by 1985 towards the 4,500 needed. The GOB therefore proposes to establish
four more schools in temporary quarters in July 1979 to meet the requirements
up to 1980. The estimated availability of MAs is shown in Annex 7, T-26.
The project includes construction, equipment and furniture for four MA
training schools to replace the temporary premises. The project will also
include training equipment, vehicles, fellowships for teaching staff and
stipends for students. (For cost estimates, see Annex 7, T-27.)
4.32      Field Workers.  The FWWs and FWAs are front-line paramedical and
family planning workers, each serving as the main source of referral and
advice on health and family planning matters for 8,000 people. Their training
and retraining is therefore crucial. The FWWs of the Health Division were
formerly malaria workers, and thus have considerable experience in the
health services. The Health Division has already begun to retrain them,
using its own funds.
4.33      A Planning Commission evaluation of FWA training in 1977 identified
inadequate knowledge of MCH care and of complications resulting from contra-
ceptive use as the chief weaknesses. These are confirmed by another recent
evaluation conducted by PCFPD. This study also indicated that the training of
FWAs related to motivating women for acceptance of family planning was weak.
4.34      To overcome the above deficiencies, the first project provided for
the establishment of 19 FWA training teams in rented premises. Under the
second project, permanent training centers will be constructed to replace
these temporary premises.  FWAs and FPAs will receive two weeks' retraining
each year at these centers, which will also give a four week pre-service
basic training course for newly recruited fieldworkers. The training load
and utilization of facilities of the centers are shown in Annex 7, T-28, and
detailed cost estimates are given in Annex 7, T-29.



- 31 -
4.35      Evaluation.  The PCFPD's Training Unit and the Planning Commission
will: (a) continuously evaluate the training of manpower supported by the
project; (b) improve the training program and arrange for the retraining
of FWWs, FPAs, FWVs and FWAs in accordance with the evaluation.
4.36      Training of Supervisors.  The single factor most responsible for
weak service delivery is inadequate supervision due to the lack of training
of the supervisors in management and technical support to field staff. The
National Institute of Population Research and Training (NIPORT), attached
to PCFPD, is responsible for the training of managers and supervisors. It
has already arranged a few training courses for thana population control
and family planning officers. About 1,000 supervisors, comprising: (a) head-
quarters staff, (b) district and thana population control and family planning
officers, and (c) district and thana MCH/FP officers--will be trained by PCFPD
for about two weeks in supervision and management. About 50 training courses
of one to five-week duration will be organized for various categories of
management personnel. The training load is given in Annex 7, T-30. The
rented buildings which house the PCFPD training section and NIPORT are not
suitable for a large training program; the GOB proposes to build a population
training accommodation to house training sections of NIPORT and PCFPD. This
building will help centralize training and facilitate organization of large
training programs. It will also help in improving the quality of training.
The facilities, provided in the project, will include a hostel for 65 students,
common room, library and classrooms for about 80 students.
Information, Education and Motivation (IEM)
4.37      The first project initiated four types of IEM activities:  (a) use
of mass media; (b) promotion of women's development programs; (c) population
education in the schools and the use of primary school teachers as change
agents; and (d) utilization of model farmers, agricultural assistants and
organized labor to spread family planning information among village men. In
1977, the GOB, with UNFPA/UNESCO assistance, formulated a population communi-
cation strategy, covering these and other activities. The second project will
continue and slightly expand the mass media activities and the women's develop-
ment programs. The UNFPA will support the other two types of activity which
have been of interest to it.
4.38      Mass Media.  The first project:  (i) established population pro-
gram cells in Radio Bangladesh; (ii) strengthened Ministry of Information
and Broadcasting's film production capacity; (iii) provided audiovisual vans;
(iv) established a Feature Writing Bureau; and (v) trained public relations
officers. Although formal evaluation of only Radio Bangladesh has been con-
ducted, the routine evaluation of other mass media activities is encouraging.
Formal evaluation of these activities will be conducted early in the second
project period.
4.39      Radio Bangladesh has set up one central and five regional population
program cells. Radio is evidently a potent media for reaching low-income
groups. It appears that family planning programs are very popular: about
75% of the persons interviewed in a recent evaluation of radio family planning
programs reported that they listen to discussions on family planning as part



- 32 -
of a 20-minute "Happy Family Program." Reportedly, about 16% of owners of
radios are practicing contraception as compared to 7% of non-owners. 1/ The
second project will provide more equipment, vehicles, fellowships and continu-
ing operating cost for three years to enable the population program cells to:
(a) produce programs for the villages; and (b) include health education in
their program. (For details of costs, see Annex 7, T-31.)
4.40      The first project provided funds for dubbing six films in Bengali,
and staff and equipment to increase Ministry of Information and Broadcasting's
film production capacity. Eleven new films have been produced towards the
target of 14 set in the first project. Most of the laboratory equipment has
arrived, but cannot be fully used until a new laboratory building has been
completed. The second project provides additional equipment, vehicles, tech-
nical assistance and operating cost which will be needed when the new labora-
tory is ready in August 1979 and when the Ministry begins to produce films on
health education, either separately or in combination with population educa-
tion (Annex 7, T-32).
4.41      Films are popular among rural people, and thousands attend each
show. The first project provided eight closed door and eight open-air
cinema vans. The eight closed-door vans are operative, and by January 1978
had given 1,760 film shows in eight pilot thanas. The eight open-air vans
have arrived and are being put into service. The second project will increase
the number of vans to 26 and provide six film motor launches to cover riverine
areas, equipment, fellowships and operating expenses (Annex 7, T-33).
4.42      The Feature Writing Bureau has a staff of ten and has published
over 500 articles on population. The second project includes operating
cost to continue the operation of the Bureau for three years (Annex 7, T-34).
To coordinate the activities, the Office of the Population Program Officer,
established under the first project, will be continued and strengthened
through operating cost support, equipment, fellowships and vehicles (Annex 7,
T-35).
4.43      Folk media in the form of wandering bands of singers and musicians
are a very important attraction in the villages, and by allying them with a
film show and a speaker, the family planning program gains credit for a whole
evening's entertainment. The project, to be implemented by the IEM Unit of
PCFPD, includes provision of allowances for holding folk festivals, training
of talents, evaluation of films, records and puppet shows (Annex 7, T-36).
4.44      Women's Development Programs. The first project supported mothers'
clubs, women's cooperatives and a women's vocational training program with a
view to raising the socio-economic status of women and to making them more
receptive to the small family norm. The schemes covered various target groups
and geographic areas, and were conducted on a pilot basis in selected villages
in about 42 thanas. The approaches of the three schemes also differed as
described later. These approaches will be reviewed in March 1980 in the light
of evaluation being conducted now before expanding the schemes under the
1/   Government of Bangladesh, Ministry of Information and Broadcasting,
Evaluation of Family Planning Motivational Program of Radio Bangladesh,
March 1979.



- 33 -
second project from July 1980. An attempt will be made to make the schemes
self-financing and commercially viable. In this respect women's cooperatives
propose to enter into an arrangement with commercial banks for obtaining loans.
4.45       The mothers' clubs are being organized by the Ministry of Labor and
Social Welfare, while women's cooperatives are part of the Integrated Rural
Development Program (IRDP) of the Ministry of Local Government, Rural Develop-
ment and Cooperatives. The women's vocational training program is being
conducted by the Women's Rehabilitation and Welfare Foundation under the
Women's Affairs Division.
4.46       A consultant engaged by IDA to review the schemes reported that,
although the three women's development schemes are not yet fully under way,
they have: (a) created jobs for women in rural areas, thus providing a
viable alternative to early motherhood and high fertility; (b) revealed a
large latent demand for family planning; (c) identified some of the problems
in delivery of family planning and health services; and (d) provided training
in leadership which has enabled women to become members of union councils,
where they voice the needs of rural women and their families.
4.47       A Planning Commission study, which compared women in one of the
schemes with women outside, concluded that: (a) the project women knew more
about contraception and practiced contraception more; members actively per-
suaded other village women to use contraception; and (b) project women made
more profits from poultry raising and vegetable growing. About 25% and 38% of
the participants in the mothers' clubs and women's cooperatives respectively
had accepted family planning. A UN study 1/ reveals that there has been a
fertility reduction of 48% among members of mothers' clubs. The study con-
cludes that it is testimony to the fact that population messages delivered
in the context of socio-economic programs has a better chance of successfully
reaching the target audience. The targets and achievements of different
women's development schemes during the first project and the proposed tar-
gets for the second project are given in Annex 7, T-37.
4.48       UNICEF 2/ and Planning Commission evaluations of the pilot schemes
noted the need to: (a) base the choice of goods to be produced on market sur-
veys; (b) organize the marketing of goods; (c) give priority to agricultural
projects for local markets; (d) maintain quality control; and (e) organize
vocational training, functional literacy, health, nutrition and population
education programs, making use of simple training materials for local needs.
4.49       The second project will:  First, provide operating cost, vehicles
and technical assistance to establish a marketing unit in PCFPD to make market
surveys, provide design and quality guidance, and make sales contracts. The
1/   Ahmadullah Mia, "Innovative Social Welfare Community Development
Approaches to Family Planning." United Nations, Social Development
Division, New York, January 1978. Quoted from Social Welfare Services
in Bangladesh, Department of Social Welfare, July 1978.
2/   Gerard, R. and al (UNICEF), "Feasibility Study of Income-Generating
Activities for Women in Bangladesh," Dacca, September 1977.



- 34 -
marketing unit will serve the three ministries which implement the women's
programs. Second, the project will provide equipment, vehicles, incremental
salaries, and materials for the formation of mothers' clubs and women's
cooperatives in 21 additional thanas, and women's vocational training programs
in 12 additional thanas. The project also includes a buffer fund for the
purchase of goods produced by mothers' clubs pending their sale in the market,
and the construction of production centers in 12 thanas for the women's voca-
tional training program. The recurring cost of mothers' clubs established
under the first project will be taken over by the GOB when first project
supp)rt ends. The second project will, however, continue to provide the
operating cost of the women's cooperatives and women's vocational training
programs started under the first project for three more years starting in
July 1980 when the first project support ends (Annex 7, T-38-41).
4.50      Women's Cooperatives.  Up to October 1978, over 500 Women's
Cooperative Societies have been established with a membership of 18,000 women
as against the target of 300, under the first project. The scheme was '
expected to cover 10 villages in each of the 19 pilot thanas, but because of
popular demand, the IRDP extended the scheme to another nine thanas and
covered 20 instead of 10 villages in each thana. Village women have deposited
over Tk 500,000 (about US$30,000) in cooperatives as a result of the schemes.
No firm data on income are available, but an income of Tk 80 to 100 per month
is reported by various members. The repayment of loans from the cooperatives
is prompt. The IRDP proposes to increase the the coverage from 20 villages per
thana to 30 and to increase the number of thanas to 40 during the second
project. Since the activities in nine additional thanas started recently
these are considered as new thanas under the second project. Thus, there
are 21 additional thanas under the second project. The number of cooperative
societies will be increased from 500 in 1978 to 1,200, with a membership of
about 48,000, in 1983.
4.51      Mothers' Clubs:  Up to October 1978, 750 mothers' clubs with a
membership of about 26,400, had been established. Mothers' clubs also offer
vocational training to women of differing backgrounds, provide them with
materials to produce goods and arrange for marketing. Most of the goods
produced by mothers' clubs are either for export or the urban market, while
women's cooperatives concentrate on agro-based activities catering to the
local market. The first project supported mothers' clubs in 40 villages in
each of the 19 thanas. The same pattern will be followed in the 21 additional
thanas of the second project,so that by the end of the second project there
will be 1,600 mothers' clubs with a membership of about 48,000.
4.52      Women's Vocational Training Program.  The Women's Rehabilitation
Welfare Foundation under the Women's Affairs Division organizes vocational
training programs for women. These training programs are of longer duration
than those of the women's cooperatives and mothers' clubs, and range from six
months to one year. Trainees receive a small stipend during the training
period. Under the first project, 40 training centers were opened in eight
thanas; these have trained 2,400 women who have motivated 45,000 women to
accept family planning.



- 35 -
4.53      Under the second project, the scheme will be extended to 12 new
thanas. The 12 thanas will be selected in common with women's cooperatives as the
the centers will train trainees for these cooperatives. As in the first
project, training centers will be established in 48 unions in these thanas.
The scheme will thus cover 20 thanas (8 old and 12 new) and 80 unions. In
order to make the schemes financially viable, production will be oriented more
towards items of local demand.
4.54      Training Community Leaders.  The power of social pressure to promote
acceptance of family planning has been proved by the success of programs using
community leaders under various pilot schemes. The IEM Unit of PCFPD will
train formal and informal community leaders such as union council chairmen,
religious leaders and school teachers, who could play a vital role in estab-
lishing linkages between program workers and target audiences and in influenc-
ing target audiences. The project provides operating expenses for the orienta-
tion of these leaders at district, thana and union levels, for curriculum
development, and evaluation. About 25,500 leaders are expected to receive
three days' orientation during the project period. (For estimates, see
Annex 7, T-36.)
4.55      Health Education.  The provision of health education for the people
is a pre-requisite to the success of any preventive or curative health service.
Health education in Bangladesh is weak. There is a small Bureau of Health
Education at Health Division headquarters. This Bureau, with its present number
of staff, is incapable of using existing manpower and facilities of MOHPP and
of other ministries for promoting health education. Each district has one
health education officer but there is no specialist staff below that level.
The second project will provide the Bureau with equipment, vehicles, technical
assistance and operating cost to formulate and implement a multi-sectoral
health education program. (For cost estimates, see Annex 7, T-42.) The
objectives of the program will be: (a) to train and equip FWWs to organize
community health education; (b) to introduce health education into the school
system; and (c) to use mass media for promotion of health education. The
Planning Commission will evaluate health education activities.
Evaluation and Research
4.56      Research and evaluation activities in population and health can be
divided into three groups: (a) those which are directly relevant to the GOB
program; (b) those which provide results of value for development planning;
and (c) those with little immediate relevance to policy-making.
4.57      The second project, like its predecessor, will be concerned only
with activities in group (a). Activities in group (b), will be sponsored by
the proposed Population and Development Cell in the General Economic Section
of the Planning Commission, which is to be funded by UNFPA. Activities in
group (c), will be sponsored by the universities and research institutions.
4.58      Research and Evaluation Capacity.  The present system of evaluation
needs to be strengthened. The Research, Evaluation, Statistics and Planning
(RESP) Unit of PCFPD is responsible for setting up a management information



- 36 -
system. A Service Statistics Cell was established in RESP, under the first
project. The External Evaluation Unit (EEU), created under the first project
in the Health and Population Planning Section of the Planning Commission, is
responsible for evaluating the impact of project and program investments.
Each of the sectoral ministries participating in the multi-sectoral IEM
program was provided funds under the first project for collecting routine
data. The Bangladesh Institute of Development Studies carries out basic
demographic and population research and evaluation studies requiring sophis-
ticated methodology. PCFPD recently decided to make the newly created NIPORT
responsible for coordinating population-related research and evaluation. The
Bureau of Statistics is responsible for census and vital registration. The
Health Information Unit of the Health Division is responsible for collecting
health program management data. All the agencies mentioned above have limited
capacity to conduct evaluation and research studies. Therefore, IDA consul-
tants 1/ reviewed the capacity of institutions and universities outside Dacca
to undertake evaluation and research studies on a contract basis. The consul-
tants prepared an inventory of institutions with capacity to undertake these
studies and a list of priority evaluation and research studies.
4.59      Four major areas of importance in evaluation and research are:
(a) setting up of a service statistics system; (b) evaluation of health and
family planning service delivery and training of manpower; and (c) evaluation
of the IEM program including community participation schemes; and (d) deter-
minants of fertility and contraceptive behavior. The second project will
strengthen the service statistics cell of the RESP Unit of PCFPD for setting
up the service statistics system. The EEU of the Planning Commission will
conduct evaluation studies defined under (b) and (c). The Bangladesh Institute
of Development Studies has already started the studies under (d).
4.60      RESP Unit.  The Service Statistics Cell of the Unit has already
initiated a new system of service statistics to replace the existing system.
The existing system has never been operative throughout the country and
reports are known to have been incomplete, often inaccurate and, in some
instances, falsified. Forms are unwieldy and complex and make no reference
to data, direct or indirect, on contraceptive prevalence.
4.61      A new system was therefore devised early in 1978.  The forms are
considerably simplified and yet will hold more information than the present
form. The FPAs collect the summary forms from the FWAs and mail them once
a month to RESP. The RESP will store the records in a mini computer now on
order. From the data stored in the computer, routine tables will be prepared
monthly and sent back to district offices for their information and use. This
feedback system is crucial for improving the program. Accuracy in the basic
field worker records depends on the proper training and supervision of the
FWAs visiting the households and on the spot checking of their reports by
their immediate supervisors, and by headquarters staff.
1/   Population Counrzil, New York.



- 37 -
4.62       The new system has been tested in seven unions, and is now being
expanded to cover seven thanas. It is planned to extend the system in the
next year to 50 unions in 50 thanas where prevalence studies will be conducted
to evaluate the system. The system is expected to be established throughout
the country in three to five years.
4.63       RESP recently made a first attempt to evaluate the new system by
comparing service statistics produced by the new system with data obtained
through direct interview. The findings are promising. For example, the
finding that more than 80% of the respondents interviewed confirmed that an
FWA had visited them in the past three months suggests that a supervised
reporting system with feedback encourages field workers to make household
visits. The FWA reports showed the contraceptive prevalence rate was 21.8%,
compared with the 19.7% found by the independent survey. On the other hand,
FWAs seem to have missed at least half the births. This suggests the need for
more intensive training and household probing to capture vital events. The
RESP Unit will make annual prevalence surveys as an independent check on FWA
reports.
4.64       The service statistics system, now being set up, has all the makings
of a vital registration system because it plans to record all changes--births
and deaths as well as migration--in household composition. Once operational,
the service statistics could be made available to another agency, say the
Bureau of Statistics, for computing vital rates and other statistics.
4.65       The project provides operating cost, equipment and technical assis-
tance for the Service Statistics Cell for the second project period. (For
cost estimates, see Annex 7, T-43.)
4.66       External Evaluation Unit (EEU).  This unit, set up in the Planning
Commission in 1976 under the first project, is responsible for the external
evaluation of the sectoral and multi-sectoral population programs.
4.67       The EEU has completed the following four studies:  (a) an evalua-
tion of the performance of FPAs and FWAs; (b) a study of family planning
habits among the staff of the Ministry of Planning; (c) an evaluation of
the FWA training program; and (d) an acceptors follow-up survey.   It is at
present undertaking follow-up surveys of the seven IEM pilot schemes of the
first project.
4.68       The EEU has a small professional staff and disproportionately large
number of interviewers. There is at present a serious lack of senior profes-
sionals with experience in sample survey research, and the prospect of finding
*         more in the near future is not bright.  The Unit should, therefore, contract
out the evaluation studies to universities or research institutions. Further,
it should concentrate on evaluating the service delivery system in general and
the performance of FWAs, FPAs and FWVs in particular. The project provides
additional senior staff, vehicles, equipment, technical assistance and funds
for forming a Service Delivery Evaluation Cell in this Unit. The staff,
supported through the first project, should be used for evaluation of IEM
activities. (For cost estimates see Annex 7, T-44.)



- 38 -
4.69      Bangladesh Institute of Development Studies.  It is at present
engaged, inter alia, in a study entitled "Determinants of Reproductive
Behavior in Bangladesh," a large-scale population research project funded
under the first project. The field investigation for the study has been
combined with that of another study entitled "Participation of Low Income
Strata in Rural Development" partially funded by ESCAP.  The study is ex-
pected to be completed by the end of 1980.
4.70      Coordination of Evaluation and Research.  The coordinating functions
of research and evaluation will be entrusted to NIPORT. It will be impossible
for a few full-time professionals in the research wing of NIPORT to coordinate
research without the assistance of a committee representing the relevant dis-
ciplines and institutions. The committee should also include representatives
from PCFPD, the University Grants Committee and the Social Science Research
Council. Agreement was reached, during negotiations, that the GOB shall form
a research and evaluation committee and prepare in consultation with the Asso-
ciation a comprehensive plan of action for evaluating the population and
health programs and the project including its various components by December
31, 1979 and shall cause such plan to be carried out in accordance with its
terms. This plan will: (a) define the measurable objectives of each compo-
nent; (b) identify corresponding quantitative performance indicators;
(c) ascertain the availability of baseline values of these indicators; (d)
determine the data to be collected; and (e) identify agencies for carrying out
specific studies. An illustration of performance indicators and timetable for
evaluation studies are given in Annexes 4 and 5.
4.71      Health Information Unit.  The health information system in Bangladesh
is in an early stage of development. The GOB has recently sanctioned a scheme
which calls for expansion of the present Health Information Unit of the Health
Division by establishing a four-man statistical cell in each of the 19 dis-
tricts and by posting a statistical assistant in each of the 412 thanas. The
Health Information Unit proposes to liaise closely in its work with the Office
of the Director of Health Services (preventive) who receive reports from FWWs,
with the RESP Unit of PCFPD and with other agencies involved in research and
evaluation work.  The Unit will combine epidemiological reporting forms with
FWWs reporting forms. The Unit will collect and analyze basic information nec-
essary for management of the health program such as: (a) manpower and equip-
ment availability and use; (b) epidemiological surveillance; and (c) results
of innovative schemes adopted in the health sector. In addition, three
medical audit teams will be formed under the Unit to note, and determine the
reasons for, under-utilization or shortage of manpower, beds, physical facili-
ties and equipment of THCs and hospitals. The teams will consist of one
medical officer, one statistical assistant and two typists. Two teams will be
assigned to THCs and FWCs and one to hospitals. The project provides salaries,
vehicles, and operating expenses for medical audit teams, data processing
equipment and fellowships. WHO will provide technical assistance. (For cost
estimates, see Annex 7, T-45.)



- 39 -
Support to Private Sector and Innovative Activities
4.72      The rigid budgets of national agencies make it difficult for them
to allocate funds to private agencies or for the testing of new ideas. The
first project therefore allocated US$350,000, later increased to $645,000, for
private sector and innovative activities (Annex 7, T-46).  PCFPD appointed
a subvention committee to review proposals for use of this fund. PCFPD has
found the fund very useful and has supported some important schemes. The
schemes supported through the project include: (a) support to voluntary agen-
cies through Bangladesh Family Planning Association; (b) intensive family
planning in selected self-reliant villages; and (c) use of folk media for
family planning in villages. Under the first of these schemes, representa-
tives from voluntary organizations at district and thana level are being
trained to organize the family planning program, for which contraceptives
are supplied. Under the second scheme about 71 self-reliant villages were
provided with small amounts of money to organize family planning related
activities in their villages. The third scheme combines folk shows with
talks by public leaders on family planning. The self-reliant village program
has been evaluated and the results are given in paras. 2.12-2.13. Other
programs will be evaluated during the second project period.
4.73      The second project includes $2 million for these activities.  This
amount, as under the first project, will be used for private sector and
innovative activities. The subvention committee set up under the first
project will review the proposals and submit them for IDA approval. About
$1 million of these funds is expected to be used for intensifying the family
planning activities of the self-reliant village programs in 700 selected
unions. Such intensification will begin after evaluation of the self-reliant
villages programs in July 1980 using the first project funds.
V.  PROJECT COST, FINANCING, IMPLEMENTATION AND EVALUATION
A. Cost
5.01      The total project cost, including contingencies, local taxes
and duties, is estimated at US$110 million. The foreign exchange component
is estimated at US$36.9 million, i.e., 34% of the total project cost. Taxes
and duties are estimated at US$9.2 million. Construction accounts for
nearly 50% of the cost. Operating cost accounts for 22% (US$24.3 million) of
the project cost. Of this, 68% or US$16.5 million is incremental operating
cost and the remaining US$7.8 million or 32% is for continuation of crucial
first project activities. The cost estimates are summarized in the following
table, while details are given in Annex 7, Tables 47-48.
