5.03                The foreign exchange component of the cost of civil works is esti-
mated at 35.2%. Vehicles, equipment and technical assistance involve foreign
exchange costs, and the operating cost (salaries, travel allowances and
maintenance of equipment and vehicles) involves local costs except for petro-
leum, which is entirely imported. Some materials, drugs and fuel are also
imported and their foreign exchange component is estimated at US$5.2 million
out of a total of US$8.5 million.
5.04                The contingency allowance of US$18.5 million covers:  (a) unfore-
seen physical factors, estimated at 10% of base cost of civil works component;
and (b) price escalation, estimated at 14% of project costs and physical
contingencies. Contingencies were calculated on the basis of estimated
expenditure and expected annual percentage increases in prices as follows:
construction, professional fees and furniture--7.5% in 1979-80 and 7.0%
annually in the remaining three years; vehicles, equipment, materials, tech-
nical assistance and operating cost--6.5% in 1979-80 and 6.0% annually in
subsequent three years.



- 42 -
B. Financing
5.05      The total project cost (US$110 million) is to be financed by an
IDA credit of US$32 million equivalent, other external financing of about
US$67 million equivalent and a GOB contribution of US$11 million equivalent,
representing about 10% of project cost. A proposed donor financing plan is
described in Annex 7, T-49. The co-financiers of the first project, Australia,
Canada, Federal Republic of Germany, Norway, Sweden, and the United Kingdom,
have agreed in principle to participate in the financing. In addition, the
Netherlands has also agreed to finance parts of the project.
All the cofinanciers except Sweden, will finance specific components.  Sweden
will, as in the first project, enter into joint financing arrangements directly
with the GOB. IDA, as the lender of last resort, will finance project compo-
nents not selected by other donors. Conclusion of arrangements satisfactory
to the Association to obtain external assistance amounting to not less than
US$67.2 million equivalent for purposes of carrying out the project is a
condition of credit effectiveness.
5.06      The five co-financiers (excepting Sweden) of the first project have
also agreed to support the salary of a population expert in the Bank's Dacca
Office, as under the first project. In order to improve the monitoring of the
project, the population expert is to be assigned an assistant whose salary
will be funded by the co-financiers and IDA.
C. Disbursements
5.07      Funds from the credit and any other funds which IDA may be author-
ized to disburse on behalf of other donors will be disbursed on the basis
of satisfactory documentation submitted by the Project Finance Officer
(details are given in Annex 6). Normal documents required by the Association
will be submitted for all items of expenditures except FWCs, salaries of local
staff, travel allowance and maintenance of vehicles. Disbursements against
these items will be made on the basis of a certificate of expenditure, the
documentation of which will not be submitted for review, but will be retained
by the GOB and available for inspection by the Association during the course
of a project supervision mission. Certificate of expenditure for the con-
struction of FWCs will be issued by the FWC Administrative Unit on the basis
of certificates of satisfactory progress by the architectural consultants.
The reimbursement will be made in installments: 50% will be paid on comple-
tion of the FWC up to plinth level, 25% on completion up to roof level, and
the remaining 25% when the FWC is put into operation, as certified by the
Secretary of PCFPD. The disbursement schedule is given in Annex 7, T-50.
5.08      The project components to be financed out of the proceeds of the
Credit include: (a) construction, furniture, equipment, and professional
fees for 434 FWCs and 13 THCs; (b) 253 ambulances for THCs, 26 four-wheel
drive vehicles for use in field supervision, and 2,000 bicycles for FWAs;
(c) vehicles, furniture, equipment and operating cost for project implemen-
tation units; and (d) unallocated amount for support to private sector and



- 43 -
innovative activities.  The percentage of the cost of the various categories
of expenditure to be met from the credit are:  (a) 90% of cost of civil works
and 100% of foreign expenditures of directly imported construction material;
(b) 100% of foreign expenditures of directly imported equipment and vehicles
and 60% of locally procured equipment and 50% of locally procured vehicles;
(c) 90% of total cost of furniture; (d) 100% of cost of consultancy services;
and (e) 100% of operating costs and support to private sector and innovative
activities.
D. Procurement
5.09      The procurement of civil works items, other than union family welfare
centers and the twelve Women's Vocational Training Centers (WVTC) will be
based on bid packages prepared in consultation with IDA. The bid packages
will possibly total 44 in number with a total cost of about $20.4 million
and will be procured by international competitive bidding (ICB) in accordance
with the Bank Guidelines, domestic contractors being accorded a preference of
7.5%. Procurement for the twelve WVTCs, which will cost about $250,000 in
total, will be on the basis of local competitive bidding procedures, under
which foreign contractors would not be excluded, and which are satisfactory
to IDA. In all cases, the contractors will be pre-qualified.
5.10      The 700 union FWCs, each estimated to cost about US$46,000, and
altogether US$32.2 million, are too scattered and numerous for the normal
procedures to be applicable.  Moreover, it is desirable that the community
identifies itself with the construction of the centers from the very beginning
to ensure that the community will subsequently maintain and make use of the
centers. The GOB will therefore appoint union project committees, each to
be responsible for the construction of the local FWC, using local resources,
with technical support from the architectural consultants. This approach
has been successfully applied to the construction of 80 pilot FWCs in the
first project. Materials such as cement, steel, bricks and sand, will be
procured locally by prudent shopping by the committees, who will also hire
construction labor. Simple furniture, worth about US$0.5 million in total,
will also be procured by prudent local shopping. Medical equipment, worth
about US$2.5 million in total, will be procured by MOHPC through ICB in
accordance with Bank Guidelines.
5.11      Contracts for furniture, (except for FWCs), equipment and vehicles,
amounting to about US$16.1 million, will be grouped in appropriate bid packages
and awarded after ICB in accordance with Bank Guidelines. In the evaluation
of bids, domestic manufacturers will be accorded a preference margin of 15%,
or the applicable custom duties if lower. Contracts for equipment and furni-
ture, which cannot be reasonably grouped in packages worth at least US$100,000,
will be awarded after local competitive bidding in accordance with GOB pro-
curement procedures, which have been found to be acceptable to IDA.



- 44 -
5.12      The project includes US$3.5 million for the purchase of material
for the production of films, filmstrips, transparencies, and printed, training
and raw materials. These items cannot be packaged in lots suitable for inter-
national competitive bidding. Foreign suppliers of such materials are well
represented in Bangladesh, and contracts will be awarded through local com-,
petitive bidding procedures, similar to those adopted for the first project,
which are satisfactory to IDA. It is proposed that drugs and dietary supple-
ment kits worth about US$4.0 million may be procured through UNICEF or through
any other agency following ICB procedures in accordance with the guidelines.
5.13       In order to provide for efficiency and flexibility in the procurement
of smaller items, contracts for equipment, furniture, vehicles and materials
for amounts not exceeding US$20,000 may be procured through international or
local shopping on the basis of at least three price quotations provided the
aggregate of such contracts does not exceed US$200,000. It is estimated that
about US$100,000 worth of these items will be procured in this manner. These
limitations do not apply to the procurement of materials and furniture for
FWCs, which are described in paragraph 5.08.
E. Revolving Fund
5.14      Under the first project, in order to speed up project payments,
GOB established a revolving fund in takas. During the second project, this
Revolving Fund will be retained. The GOB has agreed during negotiations:
(a) to maintain a Revolving Fund in a commercial Bank to be used exclusively
to make payments in takas for expenses of items of the project, excluding:
(i) salaries; (ii) travel expenses and per diem of local staff; and
(iii) import duties and taxes for all imported items for the project; (b) to
deposit in the Revolving Fund at least once a month an amount sufficient to
maintain a balance of TK 1 million; and (c) to authorize the Project Finance
Officer to make withdrawals from the Revolving Fund.
F. Implementation
5.15      The second project will be implemented by essentially the same
agencies as the first. PCFPD will coordinate project activities and a full-
time Project Coordinator will be appointed (para 5.29). The GOB proposes to
retain and strengthen the units set up to implement the first project, such
as: (a) BPDU for civil works; (b) population program offices in the four
ministries (excluding Health) participating in the IEM program; and (c) the
Project Finance Cell which will form a part of the Project Coordinator's
Office under PCFPD. In addition to the above, however, and because of the
large number of FWCs to be built and the unusual procedure for construction,
GOB proposes to create a further unit within PCFPD to administer the construc-
tion of FWCs (Annex 7, C-4).



- 45 -
5.16      BPDU.  The GOB proposes to strengthen BPDU to enable it to handle
the large civil works program included in the project with support from the
consortium of consultants similar to that provided in the first project.
The BPDU is responsible for the planning and design of all civil works
including FWCs but its monitoring and supervision activities will exclude
FWCs. It is responsible for instructing the consultants, checking the
designs and passing these to the Public Works Department for tendering action
and at all stages it monitors the progress of work. It also has responsibil-
ities in the preparation of specifications and tender documents for the
procurement of furniture and equipment. It is essential that the Director of
the Unit should be a senior person with wide administrative and technical
abilities strong enough to ensure speedy action by the Public Works Department
and other agencies. The responsibilities of BPDU vis-a-vis the Health and
PCDFP Divisions are shown in Annex 7, C-4.
5.17      The BPDU was inadequately staffed and equipped for the first project,
and needs a strong director, more staff, office accommodation, telephones, and
transport if it is to discharge its additional responsibilities under the
second project. The second project will provide BPDU with salary support for
four years, furniture, equipment and transport (Annex 7, T-51) and the GOB
will ensure the availability of adequate office accommodation and other
facilities. The consortium of local and foreign consultants set up under
the first project has worked well and there are many advantages in retaining
this arrangement. The strengthening of BPDU through the appointment of a
full-time Director, two additional executive engineers and the architectural/
engineering consultants, is a condition of credit effectiveness.
5.18      FWC Administration Unit.  This new unit under the control of the
PCFPD will deal solely with the establishment of FWCs, together with all
administrative and financial matters related thereto. The functions will
include responsibility for initiating and following up the acquisition of
land; the construction of the centers; procurement of equipment; monitoring
the purchase of furniture; and making the centers operational initially. The
new unit will issue instructions to Deputy Commissioners, Thana Population
Control and Family Planning Officers, and Union Council Chairmen to initiate
and expedite action, overcome delays, and allocate funds. It will be headed
by a senior Engineering Adviser and be staffed by senior administrative
officers. The staff will also include 20 assistant engineers stationed in
the field who will assist Deputy Commissioners and the PCFPD Deputy Directors
in monitoring the activities of the Project Committees and liaise with the
consultants. As in the first project, the GOB will engage a consortium of
architectural consultants to provide technical assistance and monitor and
certify the work of the Project Committees. The engagement of such consul-
tants is a condition of credit effectiveness. The project provides
vehicles, equipment, salary support and other operating expenses for this
unit for four years.
5.19      Public Works Department.  As in the first project, GOB proposes
that construction contracts, except for FWCs, shall be awarded and supervised
by the Public Works Department. After designs and specifications have been
prepared by the consultants and examined by BPDU, the Department will invite



- 46 -
tenders, evaluate bids and, on the approval of PCFPD, which will consult IDA,
award the contracts and supervise the work. To ensure the speedy processing
of tender documents and contracts, the GOB will set up a Population Project
Cell within the headquarters of the Public Works Department for the first two
years of project duration. The Cell will contain a superintending engineer
with an assistant engineer and an estimator for whom the project will provide
salary support (Annex 7, T-51).
5.20      Supplies Directorate.  To expedite procurement of equipment and vehi-
cles valued at about US$14 million under the project, the Supplies Directorate
needs to be strengthened. The project provides two additional Deputy Directors
for Supply and Inspection Wings and furniture and equipment (Annex 7, T-51).
5.21      FWC Project Committee.  As indicated above (para. 4.14), GOB pro-
poses to make local communities responsible for the construction of FWCs.
Thana Site Selection Committees will select and purchase sites, and Union
Project Committees will organize construction. The Chairman of the Union
Council will be a member of the site selection committee as well as Chairman
of the Union Project Committee.
5.22      The Union Project Committees will purchase materials, engage and
pay labor, and supervise construction, which will follow a pattern prescribed
in a pictorial building manual. This manual includes schedules of building
materials, furniture and equipment required, costs and simple building in-
structions. The PCFPD will engage consultants to give technical advice to
the Union Project Committees and certify the progress of work. This system
has been tried in the pilot scheme under the first project with encouraging
results. The experience gained will be used to improve methods in the second
project.
5.23      Since Union Councils are not in a position to finance, even tempo-
rarily, the purchase of sites and building materials for FWCs, the GOB pro-
poses to make them an initial advance of 50% of the estimated total cost.
Further advances will be made on evidence of proper use of the initial advance
and certificates by the consultants that construction has reached prescribed
levels. GOB will have to be prepared to reimburse justified cost overruns.
5.24      Sites.  One reason for the slow progress of civil works under the
first project was the delay in acquiring sites, although the first project
required only 45 sites, while the second requires 755. The locations for
700 FWCs and other physical facilities have been identified by the GOB.
Acquisition of sites for the buildings to be constructed in the first year
of the project as set forth in the master plan, is a condition of credit
effectiveness. Agreement on a timetable for the acquisition of the remaining
sites was reached during negotiations.
5.25      Population Program Officers.  The offices of Population Program
Officers in the Ministries of Labor and Social Welfare (Director of Social
Welfare); Local Government, Rural Development and Cooperatives; Ministry of
Information and Broadcasting; and the Women's Affairs Division will be pro-
vided with additional staff and vehicles through the project. These officers
have been upgraded wherever necessary so that they can report directly to
their Directors.



- 47 -
5.26      The first project required the Health Division to designate a
Joint Population Program Officer responsible for the execution of Health
Division project components. No officer has been formally designated for
this purpose, although various officers have handled project matters. It is
essential that the Health Division should designate a population program
officer and provide supporting staff for the execution of its share of the
first and second projects.
5.27      Project Finance Cell (PFC).  The performance of the PFC under the
first project has been unsatisfactory with respect to all its functions, such
as the submission of withdrawal applications, keeping project accounts and
routine auditing. The situation has recently improved. An accounting firm
hired by GOB to help to set up the accounting system has made preliminary
recommendations.
5.28      Auditing.  Under the first project, an audit of local currency
expenditures was required within six months of each fiscal year. Government
auditing has been delayed. Under the project the PFC will audit local currency
expenditures and reimbursement in the third quarter will depend on the audit
of expenditures in the first quarter of the fiscal year. This auditing will
be in addition to general government audits. The project provides salary
support for the appointment of additional staff by the PFC to audit the
project accounts.
5.29      Coordination.  For coordinating non-construction activities, the
PCFPD organizes regular monthly meetings of Population Program Officers to
discuss problems they have encountered. The Project Finance Officer and the
Directors of Supplies and Inspection also attend these meetings. However, the
PCFP Directorate has played little part in coordination. Greater participation
by the IEM, evaluation, service delivery, and training units of the PCFP
Directorate would help to improve coordination. Furthermore, no full-time
officer in PCFPD has been designated to coordinate all project activities. In
view of the size of the second project, the PCFPD will designate a full-time
officer of the rank of at least Deputy Secretary to coordinate project activ-
ities.
5.30      Schedule.  The key actions to be taken by the GOB, and the detailed
implementation schedule for civil works and other project components, are
presented in Annex 4. To expedite project implementation, it is essential
to complete the master plan for project physical facilities as soon as pos-
sible. Completion of a master plan by the BPDU and the architectural consult-
ants is a condition of credit effectiveness. The plan is already under
preparation and is expected to be completed by the end of June 1979. Project
implementation cannot be initiated without approval of schemes under the
project by the Planning Commission. Approval of project evaluation proformas
by the GOB is a condition of credit effectiveness.



- 48 -
G. Project Evaluation
5.31      Immediate progress on the project will be measured against an
agreed implementation schedule (Annex 4). Interim progress will be evaluated
from quantitative indicators to be specified by PCFPD in collaboration with
EEU and the implementing agencies. An illustrative list of these indicators
is given in Annex 5. Agreement on the preparation of a plan for project
evaluation was reached during negotiations (para. 4.70).
H. Project Risk
5.32      It would be unrealistic to assume that difficulties will not arise
in the implementation of the second project. The difficulties experienced
during the first project were largely due to the delay in setting up a suit-
able organization for the population program. Although the second project
starts with the advantage of an established organization, uncertainties arise
from the proposed integration of MCH/FP with health services. Given the same
organization, and GOB's commitment to the program, the expected problems are
administrative and managerial. Difficulties in project implementation will
arise if: (a) cooperation between the Health Division and PCFPD does not
improve; (b) PCFPD does not strengthen its own administrative machinery and
improve the performance of fieldworkers and the training of FWVs and other
managerial and supervisory workers; (c) BDPU is not provided with adequate
staff including consultants and facilities; (d) the PCFPD is unable to hire
suitable architectural consultants for its FWC Administrative Unit; (e) the
local committees and thana officers responsible for the construction of FWCs
do not take adequate interest; and (f) sites are not acquired according to
the agreed schedule. If the organization is changed, additional problems
may arise as the reorganization itself may cause dislocation and disruption
through staff transfers. Despite these risks the critical nature of popula-
tion problems in Bangladesh requires continuing external support to help GOB
improve the program. The components included in the project are the minimum
essential to carry out further the activities initiated under the first
project. Furthermore, agreement on actions to reduce these risks was reached
during negotiations (paras. 2.47, 2.49, 2.50, 4.07, 5.17, 5.18, 5.24 and 5.30).
5.33      The GOB is entrusting the construction of FWCs to Union Project
Committees as the only feasible method of erecting large numbers of centers
in a short period of time. It would not be possible to do so using either
the Public Works Department or the normal contracting procedures. There are
risks involved in the system, and some problems and even failures must be
anticipated but the urgent need to bring large numbers of centers into active
service operation as quickly as possible justifies the risks. These risks are
being minimized by the engagement of an Architectural Consultant firm to
advise the committees and inspect and certify the work at defined construction
stages.



- 49 -
VI. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
6.01      The primary justification of the project lies in its potential to
improve MCH and family planning services in about 270 of the 356 rural thanas.
In these thanas, 8,000 of the project FWAs will be given better technical
support by FWVs who will be given additional improved training and provided
with transport. The FWVs, using the ambulances provided in the project, will
also organize mobile clinics in the villages of these 270 thanas and will thus
bring better MCH/FP services to outlying areas. Additional drugs supplied
under the project will help in meeting the drug requirements of these 270
thanas, and will reduce morbidity and mortality.
6.02      In 100 of the 270 thanas, health and family planning services will
be improved further by the establishment of FWCs in each union. An estimated
one-fourth of the population in these 100 thanas will thus have a fully
established health and family planning infrastructure, and the decline in
morbidity, mortality and fertility, which will be measured through baseline
and follow-up studies, is expected to be substantial.
6.03      About 100,000 women will be served by vocational training, func-
tional literacy and health and family life education programs to be provided
in pilot areas. These programs will provide earning opportunities for women
who have not hitherto been able to supplement family income. The first
project has shown that women taking part in these programs are highly recep-
tive to MCH/FP instruction imparted through the programs. The mass media
activities will continue to help in creating awareness of population and
health problems and will thus enhance the demand for health and family plan-
ning services. The geographic coverage of project activities defined in
paras. 6.01 to 6.03 are shown in Annex 7, M1 and M2.
6.04      Assuming improvement in the service delivery system, it is esti-
mated that the program will be able to recruit about four million additional
acceptors by 1983, constituting about 24% of the estimated married women
in that year (Annex 7, T-52). About 1.6 million of the additional acceptors
would be the direct result of project inputs. In addition, about 36 million
women and children (or 56% of the total women and children in the country in
1983), will be reached by MCH and other health care. Of these, about 18 mil-
lion women and children will be served through the project inputs. The
project's physical facilities will continue to serve the population beyond
1983 and will thus result in greater long-term benefits than specified above.
VII. AGREEMENTS
7.01      During negotiations, agreements were reached that the GOB shall:
(a)  implement the agreed plan of phased integration of MCH/FP with
health services and review its progress at regular intervals in
consultation with the Association (para. 2.47);



- 50 -
(b) maintain the district and national-level organizations being
set up and in the event of any significant changes in the
organization shall consult the Association and take into
account the Association's comments, if any (para. 2.49);
(c)  review the PCFPD's administrative, financial and
recruitment procedures for direct recruitment of staff
and advisers to allow speedy implementation of the
health and population programs and make appropriate
modifications to these procedures as are feasible by
December 31, 1979 (para. 2.50);
(d) review existing programs of community participation in
health and population activities, particularly population
activities in village self-reliant programs, by March 31,
1980 and on the basis of such review shall prepare, by
September 30, 1980 in consultation with the Association,
a detailed strategy to strengthen such activities
(para. 2.51);
(e) carry out the agreed plan of action for improving the
performance of FWAs and FPAs by June 30, 1980; furnish
required information on implementation of the plan to
the Association by December 31, 1980; and commencing
July 1 each year evaluate the activities and performance
of FWAs and FPAs in consultation with the Association and
furnish to the Association by October 31 each year reports
of such evaluation, and based on the results of the reports
take such corrective actions as may be satisfactory to the
Association (para. 4.07);
(f) take all actions necessary to maintain the vehicles and
equipment for the project in good working condition; in
consultation with the Association, undertake an analysis
of the system for maintenance and use of such vehicles
and equipment by December 31, 1979 and shall thereafter
take steps as may be necessary to strengthen such a system
(para. 4.08);
(g) prepare or cause to be prepared by December 31, 1979 an
operational manual for maintaining and operating FWCs
(para. 4.15);
(h) undertake the construction of FWCs in accordance with the
master plan, have progress and quality of such construction
evaluated annually by the architectural consultant, and
undertake no further construction under the project unless
such progress and quality are satisfactory to the Association
(para. 4.16);



- 51 -
(i) establish MCH/FP units in all 270 THCs in operation by
July 31, 1981 (para. 4.19);
(j) in consultation with the Association, undertake a functional
analysis of its health and population programs for deter-
mining the manpower requirements at thana level and below
by December 31, 1979 and prepare a health and population
manpower policy including a national training program by
December 31, 1980 (para. 4.24);
(k) form a research and evaluation committee and prepare in
consultation with the Association a comprehensive plan
of action for evaluating the population and health programs
and the project including its various components by December
31, 1979 and shall cause such plan to be carried out in
accordance with its terms (para. 4.70);
(1) maintain a Revolving Fund in a commercial bank to be used
exclusively to make payments in takas for expenses of
defined items of the project (para. 5.14); and
(m)  acquire all sites for project physical facilities, except
those required in the first year, according to the schedule
agreed during negotiations (para. 5.24).
7.02      As conditions of credit effectiveness, the GOB shall:
(a) conclude arrangements satisfactory to the Association to
obtain external assistance amounting to no less than US$67.2
million equivalent for purposes of carrying out the project
(para. 5.05);
(b)  employ a full-time Director and two executive engineers
for BPDU and architectural/engineering consultants for BPDU
and PCFPD (paras. 5.17 and 5.18);
(c) acquire sites required for physical facilities scheduled
for construction during the first year of the project as
set forth in the master plan (para. 5.24); and
(d) complete the master plan for the physical facilities in the
project (para. 5.30);
(e) approve project proformas for carrying the project
(para. 5.30).
7.03      With the above assurances, the project constitutes a suitable
basis for an IDA credit of US$32 million to the GOB for a term of 50 years,
including a ten year grace period.

ANNEX 6
Page 1
BANGLADESH II
STATEMENT OF EXPENDITURE:  FINAL REVIEW OF DOCUMENTS
Maintenance of Project Accounts
1.        Each implementing ministry is responsible for preparation of an
annual budget and for obtaining the approval of the schemes by the appropriate
authorities within the Government. The implementing agencies then incur the
expenditures an the approved schemes and submit the accounts regularly
to the Project Finance Cell (PFC) established under the PCFPD for the
first project. The PFC's terms of reference inter alia include maintenance
of project accounts, internal audit, submission of withdrawal applications,
monitoring of disbursement and utilization of funds.
2.        Additional accountant and administrative officers for implementing
agencies are provided under the second project to enable them to deal with the
expanded financial matters under the project. Also, a PCFPD proposal to
enlarge the staff of the PFC substantially is currently under GOB's
consideration; if approved, the additional staff will enable the PFC to
discharge its increased volume of work adequately. Present and proposed PFC
staffing patterns are described in Appendix I.
3.        A local accounting firm, hired as consultant, under the first
project to review the PFC's accounting system and procedures, recommended
measures to simplify and streamline the system. The consultant's report
is under GOB's consideration. Implementation of key recommendations of the
firm will help in expediting the submission of withdrawal applications and
maintenance of proper accounts.
Statement of Expenditures
4.        Normal required documentation will be submitted by the GOB for
all expenditures except: (a) those related to the construction and
furnishing of family welfare centers (FWCs), which will be undertaken on a
community participation basis, and (b) all the operating costs. For both
these categories of expenditures the Statement of Expenditures procedure will
be adopted. Copies of supporting documents will be kept on file at the PFC,
an- raze Rvailahle for review by supervision missions and audit teams.
5.        FWCs.  Reimbursement will be made at defined stages on the basis
of a Statement of Expenditure certifying the progress of work up to those
stages. These are:
(a) 50% reimbursement when construction reaches plinth level;
(b) 25% reimbursement when construction reaches roof level; and
(c) 25% reimbursement when center becomes operational.



- 73 -
ANNEX 6
Page 2
6.        Certificates of progress will be signed by the head of the FWC
Administration Unit of PCFPD on the basis of, and supported by, certificates
of progress given by the consultants.
7.        The statements of expenditure from the GOB will be accompanied by:
(a) a statement of expenditure on each individual FWC included in
the statement; and
(b) a certificate of progress of construction of each FWC included
in the statement signed by the head of the FWC Administration
Unit.
8.        Furniture for FWCs will be procured by the Union Construction
Committees at union level on the basis of prudent shopping and reimbursement
for such expenditure will be on the basis of statement of expenditure certified
by the FWC Administration Unit.
9'        Operating costs.  Statements of expenditures will be submitted for
all items of expenditures constituting operating costs such as salaries,
maintenance of vehicles, travel allowance and purchase of materials.
10.       The documentation and audit requirements for each category of
expenditures are summarized in the following table:
Information Required on
Categories         Statement of Expenditures       Audit Requirements
1.  Salaries       1.  Designation and grade       1.  Regular check by PFC of

Audit
11.       Ideally, all withdrawal applications for local currency expendi-
tures should be supported by the audited statement of expenditures. Under
the first project the GOB was requested to submit the audited statement
within six months of each fiscal year, but these statements were delayed.
Therefore, under the second project disbursements of local currency
expenditures in the third quarter of the fiscal year will be contingent
upon submission of audit statement of accounts for expenditures incurred
in the first quarter. Such audit reports will be prepared by the audit
team of the PFC.
12.       In accordance with GOB procedures, the General Accounting
Office will maintain overall responsibility for auditing project accounts.
In view of this Office's heavy workolad, however, the GOB has agreed
to provide separate auditing of local currency expenditures under the
project by the PFC. Additional staff for this purpose is included
in the project.

T-14
BANGALDESH II
BANGLADESH POPULATION PROGRAM RATING
Criteria /1                   Suggested Rating /2 ,
1. Fertility reduction included in official planning
policy                                                         2
2. Favorable public statements by political leaders.                 2
3. Contraception readily and easily available
publicly and commercially throughout the country.              1
4. Vigorous efforts to provide family planning
services to all married women of reproductive ages.            1
5. Adequate family planning and administration structures.           1
6. Training facilities available and utilized.                       1
7. Full-time home visiting field workers.                            1
8. Voluntary sterilization services openly and legally
available to all.                                              1
9. Government provides substantial part of family
planning budget from its own resources.                        1
10. Use of mass media on a substantial basis.                         1
11. Record-keeping systems for clients at clinic level
and for program service statistics.                             1
12. Postpartum information, education and service
available to all.                                              0
13. Serious and continuous evaluation effort.                         1
14
/1 W. Parker Mauldin and Bernard Berelson, "Conditions to Fertility
Decline in Developing Countries," 1967-75: The Population Council,
Volume 9, Number 5, May 1978, pp. 90, 128.  The score for each
criterion was two points if the answer was yes, one point if the answer
was a qualified yes, and zero if the answer was partially or no.
Ratings may range from 0-30.  Programs with 0-9 ratings were weak,
10-19 moderate, and 20 and above strong.
/2  Our suggested rating for the present Bangladesh population program.

fellowship and operating cost.
/6  GOB finances the cost of land.
/7 ODM finances the cost of fellowship; all other expenditures will be financed by GOB/SIDA.
/8 IDA finances all project expenditures except the related duties and taxes which GOB will finance.
/9  IDA finances 50% of the salary and allowances of the existing project officer stationed in the World Bank office; other donors
will finance the remaining half, plus 100% of those for the proposed Assistant.  Hence, the proportions financed by all donors
on the two officers are as follows:  GOA - 6%; CIDA - 7%; FRG - 25%; IDA - 29%; NORAD - 27%; and ODM - 6%.

BANGLADESH II
RELATED DOCUMENTS AND DATA AVAILABLE IN THE PROJECT FILE
A. General Reports and Studies Related to Population/Family Planning
A-1   Country Health Programming, Bangladesh, Information Document,
World Health Organization, Dacca, April 1977.
A-2   Nutrition Survey of Rural Bangladesh, 1975-76, Institute of
Nutrition and Food Science, University of Dacca, Dacca,
Bangladesh, December 1977.
A-3   Bangladesh Health Profile, 1977, Ministry of Health Population
Control and Family Planning (MOHPP), Government of Bangladesh.
A-4   A Comprehensive Population Communication Strategy, Bangladesh,
Ministry of Information and Broadcasting, Government of
Bangladesh, May 1977.
A-5   Bangladesh National Population Policy, June 1976.
B. General Reports and Studies Related to the Project
B-1   Procurement and Manufacture of Drugs for Use in Primary Health
Care in Bangladesh, Draft Feasibility Study Report, December 1978.
B-2   Feasibility Study of Setting Up Two-Way Radio Communication
Between Field Workers and Thana Health Complexes, November 1970.
B-3   Report on Bangladesh Population Program Research and Evaluation
by Moni Nag and Dorothy Nortman, November 1978.
B-4   Report on Women's Projects under Bangladesh Population Project-
S. A. Zeidenstein (Consultant), May 1978.
B-5   Report on Women's Programs in Bangladesh - Marketing and Design
Development, D. N. Saraf, (Consultant), May 1978.
B-6   Report on Identification of a Second Population Project, March 14, 1978.
B-7   Report on the Training Program, T. DeRosa (Consultant), January 1979.
B-8   A Close Look at the Population Programmes of the Ministry of
Information and Broadcasting, Ministry of Information and
Broadcasting, Government of Bangladesh, May 1978.
B-9   A Brief Evaluation Report of Rural Mother's Club and Rural Women's
Cooperatives Programme, Evaluation Unit of the Population Section,
Planning Commission, Government of Banglafiesh, October 1978.
B-10 A Study of Performance of Family Planning Assistants Under Family
Planning Scheme (1975-78), Evaluation Unit of the Population Section
of the Planning Commission, Government of Bangladesh.
